# Complete Guide to Parallel and Concurrent Programming with C++

> Parallel programming unlocks a program’s ability to execute multiple instructions simultaneously, increases the overall processing throughput, and is key to writing faster and more efficient applications. In this course, join instructors Barron and Olivia Chiu Stone as they introduce the basics of parallel and concurrent programming in C++, providing the foundational knowledge you need to write mo

### 1. Parallel Computing Hardware

#### Sequential vs. parallel computing
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/sequential-vs-parallel-computing?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/sequential-vs-parallel-computing?u=76281980&t=1)** - Let's start by looking at what parallel computing means and why it's useful.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/sequential-vs-parallel-computing?u=76281980&t=6)** Why it's worth the extra effort to write parallel code.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/sequential-vs-parallel-computing?u=76281980&t=9)** A computer program is just a list of instructions that tells a computer what to do, like the steps in a recipe that tell me what to do when I'm cooking.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/sequential-vs-parallel-computing?u=76281980&t=18)** Like a computer, I simply follow those instructions to execute the program.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/sequential-vs-parallel-computing?u=76281980&t=22)** So, to execute the program or recipe to make a salad, I'll start by chopping some lettuce and putting it on a plate.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/sequential-vs-parallel-computing?u=76281980&t=30)** (lettuce crunching) (knife thudding) Then I'll slice up a cucumber and add it.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/sequential-vs-parallel-computing?u=76281980&t=38)** (knife thudding) Next, I'll slice and add a few chunks of tomato.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/sequential-vs-parallel-computing?u=76281980&t=48)** (knife scratching) (knife rattling) I'll try not to cry while I slice the onion.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/sequential-vs-parallel-computing?u=76281980&t=59)** (knife thudding) (knife rattling) And finally, I add the dressing.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/sequential-vs-parallel-computing?u=76281980&t=72)** Done.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/sequential-vs-parallel-computing?u=76281980&t=73)** As a single cook working alone in the kitchen, I'm a single processor executing this program in a sequential manner.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/sequential-vs-parallel-computing?u=76281980&t=80)** The program is broken down into a sequence of discreet instructions that I execute one after another, and I can only execute one instruction at any given moment.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/sequential-vs-parallel-computing?u=76281980&t=90)** There's no overlap between them.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/sequential-vs-parallel-computing?u=76281980&t=92)** This type of serial or sequential programming is how software has traditionally been written, and it's how new programmers are usually taught to code because it's easy to understand, but it has its limitations.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/sequential-vs-parallel-computing?u=76281980&t=106)** The time it takes for a sequential program to run is limited by the speed of the processor and how fast it can execute that series of instructions.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/sequential-vs-parallel-computing?u=76281980&t=115)** I'll slice and chop ingredients as fast as I can, but there's a limit to how quickly I can complete all of those tasks by myself.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/sequential-vs-parallel-computing?u=76281980&t=123)** Each step takes some amount of time, and in total, it takes me about three minutes to execute this program and make a salad.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/sequential-vs-parallel-computing?u=76281980&t=131)** That's my personal speed record, and I can't make a salad any faster than that without help.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/sequential-vs-parallel-computing?u=76281980&t=137)** - That's my cue.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/sequential-vs-parallel-computing?u=76281980&t=138)** (board rattling) Two cooks in the kitchen represent a system with multiple processors.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/sequential-vs-parallel-computing?u=76281980&t=144)** Now we can break down the salad recipe and execute some of those steps in parallel.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/sequential-vs-parallel-computing?u=76281980&t=148)** - While I chop the lettuce. - I'll slice the cucumber.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/sequential-vs-parallel-computing?u=76281980&t=151)** (knives rattling)
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/sequential-vs-parallel-computing?u=76281980&t=155)** - And when I'm done chopping lettuce, I'll slice the tomatoes
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/sequential-vs-parallel-computing?u=76281980&t=158)** - And I'll chop the onion.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/sequential-vs-parallel-computing?u=76281980&t=160)** (knife thudding)
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/sequential-vs-parallel-computing?u=76281980&t=163)** - And finally I'll add some dressing.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/sequential-vs-parallel-computing?u=76281980&t=165)** - Hold on.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/sequential-vs-parallel-computing?u=76281980&t=168)** Now it's ready.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/sequential-vs-parallel-computing?u=76281980&t=169)** - Finally, the dressing.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/sequential-vs-parallel-computing?u=76281980&t=173)** - Working together, we broke the recipe into independent parts that can be executed simultaneously by different processors.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/sequential-vs-parallel-computing?u=76281980&t=180)** While I was slicing cucumbers and onions, Baron was chopping lettuce and tomatoes.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/sequential-vs-parallel-computing?u=76281980&t=185)** That final step of adding dressing was dependent on all of the previous steps being done, so we had to coordinate with each other for that step.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/sequential-vs-parallel-computing?u=76281980&t=193)** By working together in parallel, it only took us two minutes to make the salad, which is faster than the three minutes it took Baron to do it alone.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/sequential-vs-parallel-computing?u=76281980&t=201)** Adding a second cook in the kitchen doesn't necessarily mean we'll make the salad twice as fast, because having extra cooks in the kitchen adds complexity.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/sequential-vs-parallel-computing?u=76281980&t=210)** We have to spend extra effort to communicate with each other to coordinate our actions.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/sequential-vs-parallel-computing?u=76281980&t=215)** - And there might be times when one of us has to wait for the other cook to finish a certain step before we continue on.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/sequential-vs-parallel-computing?u=76281980&t=222)** Those coordination challenges are part of what make writing parallel programs harder than simple sequential programs.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/sequential-vs-parallel-computing?u=76281980&t=228)** But that extra work can be worth the effort because when done right, parallel execution increases the overall throughput of a program, enabling us to break down large tasks to accomplish them faster, or to accomplish more tasks in a given amount of time.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/sequential-vs-parallel-computing?u=76281980&t=245)** Some computing problems are so large or complex that it's not practical or even possible to solve them with a single computer.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/sequential-vs-parallel-computing?u=76281980&t=253)** Web search engines that process millions of transactions every second are only possible thanks to parallel computing.
>
> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/sequential-vs-parallel-computing?u=76281980&t=260)** - In many industries, the time saved using parallel computing also leads to saving money.
>
> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/sequential-vs-parallel-computing?u=76281980&t=266)** The advantages of being able to solve a problem faster often outweighs a cost of investing in parallel computing hardware.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

> **CLI Commands:** make (6)
> **Code Keywords:** finally, (2), let (1), continue (1)

#### Parallel computing architectures
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/parallel-computing-architectures?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/parallel-computing-architectures?u=76281980&t=0)** - Parallel computing requires parallel hardware with multiple processors to execute different parts of a program at the same time.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/parallel-computing-architectures?u=76281980&t=9)** But before you dive into writing software, it helps to understand how different types of parallel computers are structured.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/parallel-computing-architectures?u=76281980&t=16)** One of the most widely used systems for classifying multiprocessor architectures is Flynn's Taxonomy, which distinguishes four classes of computer architecture based on two factors: The number of concurrent instruction, or control streams, and the number of data streams.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/parallel-computing-architectures?u=76281980&t=33)** The class names are usually written as four letter acronyms that indicate whether they have single or multiple instruction streams and data streams.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/parallel-computing-architectures?u=76281980&t=43)** For example, SIMD stands for Single Instruction Multiple Data.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/parallel-computing-architectures?u=76281980&t=49)** The simplest of these four classes is the Single Instruction, Single Data, or SISD architecture, which is a sequential computer with a single processor unit.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/parallel-computing-architectures?u=76281980&t=60)** If I'm an SISD computer, at any given time, I can only execute one series of instructions, such as chopping, and I can only act on one element of data at a time, this carrot.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/parallel-computing-architectures?u=76281980&t=73)** (knife clanging)
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/parallel-computing-architectures?u=76281980&t=75)** - It's simple like an old computer.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/parallel-computing-architectures?u=76281980&t=78)** The next class in Flynn's Taxonomy is Single Instruction, Multiple Data, or SIMD, which is a type of parallel computer with multiple processing units.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/parallel-computing-architectures?u=76281980&t=90)** All of its processors execute the same instruction at any given time.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/parallel-computing-architectures?u=76281980&t=94)** But they can each operate on different data element.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/parallel-computing-architectures?u=76281980&t=98)** As an SIMD computer, our two processors are both executing the same chopping instruction, but I'm chopping celery as my data while Baron chops a carrot.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/parallel-computing-architectures?u=76281980&t=108)** - And we'll execute those instructions in sync with each other.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/parallel-computing-architectures?u=76281980&t=112)** (knives clanging) This type of SIMD architecture is well-suited for applications that perform the same handful of operations on a massive set of data elements, like image processing.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/parallel-computing-architectures?u=76281980&t=126)** And most modern computers use Graphic Processing Units, or GPUs, with SIMD instructions to do just that.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/parallel-computing-architectures?u=76281980&t=134)** Our third class is the opposite of SIMD.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/parallel-computing-architectures?u=76281980&t=138)** In a Multiple Instruction, Single Data, or MISD architecture, each processing unit independently executes its own separate series of instructions.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/parallel-computing-architectures?u=76281980&t=148)** However, all of those processors are operating on the same single stream of data.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/parallel-computing-architectures?u=76281980&t=154)** That's like Olivia executing the chopping instruction while I execute a different peeling instruction.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/parallel-computing-architectures?u=76281980&t=160)** But we're both chopping and peeling the same carrot at the same time.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/parallel-computing-architectures?u=76281980&t=166)** - Yeah, we're not doing that.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/parallel-computing-architectures?u=76281980&t=168)** - As you can see, MISD doesn't make much practical sense, so it's not a commonly used architecture.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/parallel-computing-architectures?u=76281980&t=175)** - But our fourth and final architecture is.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/parallel-computing-architectures?u=76281980&t=178)** In a Multiple Instruction, Multiple Data, or MIMD computer, every processing unit can be executing a different series of instructions, and at the same time, each of those processors can be operating on a different set of data.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/parallel-computing-architectures?u=76281980&t=193)** Now, I can slice celery while Baron peel's carrots.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/parallel-computing-architectures?u=76281980&t=199)** - MIMD is the most commonly used architecture in Flynn's Taxonomy, and you'll find it in everything from multi-core PCs to network clusters and supercomputers.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/parallel-computing-architectures?u=76281980&t=209)** Now, that broad MIMD category is sometimes further subdivided into two parallel programming models, which also have four letter names.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/parallel-computing-architectures?u=76281980&t=219)** Single Program, Multiple Data, or SPMD, and Multiple Program Multiple Data, MPMD.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/parallel-computing-architectures?u=76281980&t=228)** In the SPMD model, multiple processing units are executing a copy of the same single program simultaneously.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/parallel-computing-architectures?u=76281980&t=236)** However, they can each use different data.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/parallel-computing-architectures?u=76281980&t=240)** That might sound a lot like the SIMD architecture from earlier, but it's different because, although each processor is executing the same program, they do not have to be executing the same instruction at the same time.
>
> **[4:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/parallel-computing-architectures?u=76281980&t=254)** The processors can run asynchronously, and the program usually includes conditional logic that allows different tasks within the program to only execute specific parts of the overall program.
>
> **[4:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/parallel-computing-architectures?u=76281980&t=267)** If Olivia and I are both following the same recipe or program, I can execute part of it while Olivia's processor handles a different task.
>
> **[4:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/parallel-computing-architectures?u=76281980&t=276)** This SPMD model is the most common style of parallel programming.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/parallel-computing-architectures?u=76281980&t=281)** and when we show you programming examples later in this course, we'll structure the code as a single program and execute it on a multi-core desktop computer, which is an MIMD architecture.
>
> **[4:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/parallel-computing-architectures?u=76281980&t=292)** - Now, if each of our processors is executing a different recipe, that represents the Multiple Program, Multiple Data, or MPMD model.
>
> **[5:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/parallel-computing-architectures?u=76281980&t=302)** In this scenario, processors can be executing different independent programs at the same time while, of course, also be operating on different data.
>
> **[5:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/parallel-computing-architectures?u=76281980&t=312)** Typically, in this model, one processing node will be selected as the host or manager, which runs one program that farms out data to the other nodes running a second program.
>
> **[5:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/parallel-computing-architectures?u=76281980&t=322)** Those other nodes do their work and return their results to the manager.
>
> **[5:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/parallel-computing-architectures?u=76281980&t=326)** MPMD is not as common as SPMD, but it can be useful for some applications that lend themselves to functional decomposition, which we'll cover later on.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Env Vars:** simd (7), mimd (4), spmd (4), mpmd (3), sisd (2)
> **Speakers:** - parallel (1), - it (1), - and (1), - yeah (1), - as (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (1), find (1), node (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (2), is an  (1)
> **Analogies:** for example (1), such as (1)
> **Code Keywords:** while, (1)

#### Shared versus distributed memory
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-versus-distributed-memory?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-versus-distributed-memory?u=76281980&t=0)** - In addition to a parallel computer's architecture, which can be categorized using Flynn's taxonomy, another aspect to consider is... Uh. (snaps)
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-versus-distributed-memory?u=76281980&t=12)** - Memory.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-versus-distributed-memory?u=76281980&t=13)** It's important to understand how the memory's organized and how the computer accesses data.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-versus-distributed-memory?u=76281980&t=18)** - Right.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-versus-distributed-memory?u=76281980&t=18)** You could put a billion processors in a computer, but if they can't access memory fast enough to get the instructions and data they need, then you won't gain anything from having all those processors.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-versus-distributed-memory?u=76281980&t=30)** Computer memory usually operates at a much slower speed than processors do, and when one processor is reading or writing to memory, that often prevents any other processors from accessing that same memory element.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-versus-distributed-memory?u=76281980&t=43)** There are two main memory architectures that exist for parallel computing, shared memory and distributed memory.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-versus-distributed-memory?u=76281980&t=51)** In a shared memory system, all processors have access to the same memory as part of a global address space.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-versus-distributed-memory?u=76281980&t=58)** Although each processor operates independently, if one processor changes a memory location, all of the other processors will see that change.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-versus-distributed-memory?u=76281980&t=67)** So, if I change something in our shared memory space...
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-versus-distributed-memory?u=76281980&t=71)** - Hey, that potato is two potatoes.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-versus-distributed-memory?u=76281980&t=74)** - Every other processor sees that change too.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-versus-distributed-memory?u=76281980&t=77)** Now, the term shared memory doesn't necessarily mean all of this data exists on the same physical device.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-versus-distributed-memory?u=76281980&t=84)** It could be spread across a cluster of systems.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-versus-distributed-memory?u=76281980&t=87)** The key is that both of our processors see everything that happens in the shared memory space.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-versus-distributed-memory?u=76281980&t=92)** Shared memory is often classified into one of two categories, uniform memory access and non-uniform memory access, which are based on how the processors are connected to memory and how quickly they can access it.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-versus-distributed-memory?u=76281980&t=106)** In a uniform memory access, or UMA system, all of the processors have equal access to the memory, meaning they can access it equally fast.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-versus-distributed-memory?u=76281980&t=116)** There are several types of UMA architectures, but the most common is a symmetric multi-processing system, or SMP.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-versus-distributed-memory?u=76281980&t=125)** An SMP system has two or more identical processors, which are connected to a single shared memory, often through a system bus.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-versus-distributed-memory?u=76281980&t=134)** In the case of modern multi-core processors, which you find in everything from desktop computers to cell phones, each of the processing cores are treated as a separate processor.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-versus-distributed-memory?u=76281980&t=144)** For this course, we'll be focused on parallel programming within the SMP architecture.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-versus-distributed-memory?u=76281980&t=150)** In the example code we show you, we'll be running on a multi-core desktop computer.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-versus-distributed-memory?u=76281980&t=154)** Now in most modern processors, each core has its own cache, which is a small, very fast piece of memory that only it can see, and it uses it to store data that it's frequently working with.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-versus-distributed-memory?u=76281980&t=167)** However, caches introduce the challenge that if one processor copies a value from the shared main memory and then makes a change to it in its local cache, then that change needs to be updated back in the shared memory before another processor reads the old value which is no longer current.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-versus-distributed-memory?u=76281980&t=186)** This issue, called cache coherency, is handled by the hardware in multi-core processors, so we will not go into detail on it for this course, but it's something you should be aware of if you find yourself working with larger, more complex parallel computing systems.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-versus-distributed-memory?u=76281980&t=202)** The other type of shared memory is a non-uniform memory access, or NUMA system, which is often made by physically connecting multiple SMP systems together.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-versus-distributed-memory?u=76281980&t=214)** The access is non-uniform because some processors will have quicker access to certain parts of memory than others.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-versus-distributed-memory?u=76281980&t=221)** It takes longer to access things over the bus.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-versus-distributed-memory?u=76281980&t=223)** But overall, every processor can still see everything in memory.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-versus-distributed-memory?u=76281980&t=229)** These shared memory architectures have the advantage of being easier for programming in regards to memory because it's easy to share data between different parts of a parallel program.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-versus-distributed-memory?u=76281980&t=238)** The downside is that they don't always scale well.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-versus-distributed-memory?u=76281980&t=241)** Adding more processors to a shared memory system will increase traffic on the shared memory bus.
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-versus-distributed-memory?u=76281980&t=247)** And if you factor in maintaining cache coherency, it becomes a lot of communication that needs to happen between all the parts.
>
> **[4:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-versus-distributed-memory?u=76281980&t=254)** In addition to that, shared memory puts responsibility on the programmer to synchronize memory accesses to ensure correct behavior.
>
> **[4:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-versus-distributed-memory?u=76281980&t=262)** But, we'll look into that later.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-versus-distributed-memory?u=76281980&t=265)** - Okay, that's enough about shared memory.
>
> **[4:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-versus-distributed-memory?u=76281980&t=269)** In a distributed memory system, each processor has its own local memory with its own address space, so the concept of a global address space doesn't exist.
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-versus-distributed-memory?u=76281980&t=280)** All of the processors are connected through some sort of network, which can be as simple as ethernet.
>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-versus-distributed-memory?u=76281980&t=285)** Each processor operates independently, and if it makes changes to its local memory, that change is not automatically reflected in the memory of other processors.
>
> **[4:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-versus-distributed-memory?u=76281980&t=295)** If I make a change to the data in my memory, Baron's processor is oblivious to that change.
>
> **[5:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-versus-distributed-memory?u=76281980&t=302)** It's up to the programmer to explicitly define how and when data is communicated between the nodes in a distributed system, and that's often a disadvantage.
>
> **[5:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-versus-distributed-memory?u=76281980&t=312)** - Communication is always tough.
>
> **[5:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-versus-distributed-memory?u=76281980&t=314)** - The advantage of a distributed memory architecture is that it's scalable.
>
> **[5:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-versus-distributed-memory?u=76281980&t=319)** When you add more processors to the system, you get more memory too.
>
> **[5:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-versus-distributed-memory?u=76281980&t=323)** This structure makes it cost effective to use commodity, off the shelf computers, and networking equipment to build large distributed memory systems.
>
> **[5:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-versus-distributed-memory?u=76281980&t=333)** Most supercomputers use some form of distributed memory architecture or a hybrid of distributed and shared memory.
>
> **[5:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-versus-distributed-memory?u=76281980&t=340)** - But for this course, we'll stick with simple shared memory in an SMP architecture.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Speakers:** - in (1), - memory (1), - right (1), - hey (1), - every (1)
> **Env Vars:** smp (5), uma (2), numa (1)
> **CLI Commands:** find (2), make (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (3)
> **Best Practices:** the key is (1)
> **Warnings:** be aware (1)


### 2. Threads and Processes

#### Thread versus process
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-versus-process?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-versus-process?u=76281980&t=0)** - When a computer runs an application, that instance of the program executing is referred to as a process.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-versus-process?u=76281980&t=8)** A process consists of the program's code, its data, and information about its state.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-versus-process?u=76281980&t=14)** Each process is independent and has its own separate address space and memory.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-versus-process?u=76281980&t=20)** A computer can have hundreds of active processes at once, and an operating system's job is to manage all of them.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-versus-process?u=76281980&t=27)** - Now, within every process, there are one or more smaller sub-elements called threads.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-versus-process?u=76281980&t=33)** These are kind of like tiny processes.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-versus-process?u=76281980&t=35)** Each of those threads is an independent path of execution through the program, a different sequence of instructions, and they can only exist as part of a process.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-versus-process?u=76281980&t=46)** Threads are the basic units that the operating system manages, and it allocates time on the processor to actually execute them.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-versus-process?u=76281980&t=54)** - To conceptualize the relationship between a process and its threads, think of Olivia and I cooking together in the kitchen as being two threads executing as part of the same process.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-versus-process?u=76281980&t=65)** - We both work independently doing our own tasks that contribute to the overall execution of our program.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-versus-process?u=76281980&t=71)** For example, if we are part of a process to make a salad, my thread might handle retrieving vegetables from the pantry and fridge.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-versus-process?u=76281980&t=79)** - And I'll handle chopping them up.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-versus-process?u=76281980&t=81)** If the program requires other tasks, we might create additional threads to handle those too.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-versus-process?u=76281980&t=86)** Hey, Steve. - Hey.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-versus-process?u=76281980&t=87)** - Can you make some salad dressing?
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-versus-process?u=76281980&t=89)** - I sure can.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-versus-process?u=76281980&t=90)** - Now, our salad making process has three active threads, and when one of those threads finishes executing its instructions,
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-versus-process?u=76281980&t=97)** - All done.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-versus-process?u=76281980&t=100)** - it'll exit and leave the remaining threads to continue what they're doing.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-versus-process?u=76281980&t=104)** - Threads that belong to the same process share the process's address space, which gives them access to the same resources in memory, including the program's executable code and data.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-versus-process?u=76281980&t=115)** You can think of the kitchen that our two threads are working in like the shared address space for our process.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-versus-process?u=76281980&t=121)** We both have direct access to the same cookbooks containing our instructions or code, and the ingredients that we're cooking with represents the data and variables we're manipulating.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-versus-process?u=76281980&t=132)** The ability for both of us to use these resources is certainly convenient, and it enables us to easily work together, but it does create the potential to cause problems if we don't coordinate our actions, as we'll see later in this course.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-versus-process?u=76281980&t=146)** - Sharing resources between separate processes is not as easy as sharing between threads in the same process, because every process exists in its own address space, its own separate kitchen.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-versus-process?u=76281980&t=162)** In this process, our two threads are making a salad, but that other process next door is running a different program.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-versus-process?u=76281980&t=168)** Those threads are baking a cake.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-versus-process?u=76281980&t=171)** - Our variables and data are isolated to this address space, this kitchen, so the threads in the other process can't directly access our salad data.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-versus-process?u=76281980&t=180)** - Good, we don't want you healthy salad makers messing with our cake, either.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-versus-process?u=76281980&t=186)** - There are ways to communicate and share data between processes, but it requires a bit more work than communicating between threads.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-versus-process?u=76281980&t=195)** You have to use system-provided interprocess communication mechanisms like sockets and pipes, allocating special interprocess shared memory space, or using remote procedure calls, which is beyond the scope of what we'll be discussing in this course.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-versus-process?u=76281980&t=211)** - Now, it's possible to write parallel programs that use multiple processes working together towards a common goal, or using multiple threads within a single process.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-versus-process?u=76281980&t=222)** - So, which is better, using multiple threads or multiple processes?
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-versus-process?u=76281980&t=226)** - Well, like most things in programming, it depends.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-versus-process?u=76281980&t=229)** It depends on what you're doing and the environment it's running in, because the implementation of threads and processes differs between operating systems and programming languages.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-versus-process?u=76281980&t=239)** If your application is going to be distributed across multiple computers, you'll most likely need separate processes for that.
>
> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-versus-process?u=76281980&t=246)** But as a rule of thumb, if you can structure your program to take advantage of multiple threads, stick with using threads rather than multiple processes.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-versus-process?u=76281980&t=256)** Threads are considered lightweight compared to processes, which are more resource intensive.
>
> **[4:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-versus-process?u=76281980&t=261)** A thread requires less overhead to create and terminate than a process, and it's usually faster for an operating system to switch between executing threads from the same process than to switch between different processes.

>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-versus-process-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=42)** So the computer treats those two physical cores as four logical processors.

>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-versus-process-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=54)** Hyperthreading takes advantage of unused parts of the processor, so if one thread is paused or not using a specific resource, then the other thread may be able to use it.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-versus-process-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=65)** Under certain workloads, that can create performance improvements, but it's highly application dependent.

#### Concurrent versus parallel execution
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/concurrent-versus-parallel-execution?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/concurrent-versus-parallel-execution?u=76281980&t=1)** - Just because a program is structured to have multiple threads or processes does not mean they'll necessarily execute in parallel.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/concurrent-versus-parallel-execution?u=76281980&t=8)** A concept that's closely related to parallel execution but often gets confused with it is concurrency.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/concurrent-versus-parallel-execution?u=76281980&t=15)** Concurrency refers to the ability of an algorithm or program to be broken into different parts that can be executed out of order or partially out of order without affecting the end result.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/concurrent-versus-parallel-execution?u=76281980&t=27)** Concurrency is about how a program is structured and the composition of independently executing processes.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/concurrent-versus-parallel-execution?u=76281980&t=34)** Consider this recipe to make a salad, which includes several steps that involve slicing and chopping vegetables.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/concurrent-versus-parallel-execution?u=76281980&t=41)** We can decompose those steps into a collection of concurrent tasks because the relative order in which we do them doesn't matter.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/concurrent-versus-parallel-execution?u=76281980&t=48)** They're order independent.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/concurrent-versus-parallel-execution?u=76281980&t=50)** To keep things simple, let's just focus on two of those tasks for now.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/concurrent-versus-parallel-execution?u=76281980&t=55)** I'll chop onions.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/concurrent-versus-parallel-execution?u=76281980&t=56)** - And I'll slice cucumbers.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/concurrent-versus-parallel-execution?u=76281980&t=58)** This knife represents our computer's processor.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/concurrent-versus-parallel-execution?u=76281980&t=61)** We only have one knife, so this is a single core processor and only one of us will be able to execute our vegetable chopping routine at any given time.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/concurrent-versus-parallel-execution?u=76281980&t=70)** - We'll have to take turns, you go first.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/concurrent-versus-parallel-execution?u=76281980&t=72)** - Thanks, my thread will use the processor to execute and slice some cucumbers.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/concurrent-versus-parallel-execution?u=76281980&t=79)** Then after a bit, we'll swap places.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/concurrent-versus-parallel-execution?u=76281980&t=84)** - Now my thread gets some time to execute and slice onions.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/concurrent-versus-parallel-execution?u=76281980&t=90)** - I want to slice now.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/concurrent-versus-parallel-execution?u=76281980&t=92)** - So we'll swap places again and we'll keep on doing this until we're both done.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/concurrent-versus-parallel-execution?u=76281980&t=98)** In this scenario, we're running concurrently because our two independent processes overlap in time.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/concurrent-versus-parallel-execution?u=76281980&t=106)** However, since we only have a single processor, only one of us will actually be executing at any instant in time.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/concurrent-versus-parallel-execution?u=76281980&t=113)** If we swap places and take turns more frequently, it might create the illusion that we're executing simultaneously on our single processor, but this is not true parallel execution.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/concurrent-versus-parallel-execution?u=76281980&t=125)** - To actually execute in parallel, we need parallel hardware.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/concurrent-versus-parallel-execution?u=76281980&t=129)** In our kitchen, that means another knife and cutting board, a second processor.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/concurrent-versus-parallel-execution?u=76281980&t=133)** But in regards to computers, parallel hardware can come in a variety of forms.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/concurrent-versus-parallel-execution?u=76281980&t=138)** Most modern processors used in things like desktop computers and cell phones have multiple processing cores.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/concurrent-versus-parallel-execution?u=76281980&t=145)** Graphics processing units or GPUs contain hundreds or even thousands of specialized cores working in parallel to make amazing graphics that you see on the screen.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/concurrent-versus-parallel-execution?u=76281980&t=155)** And computer clusters distribute their processing across multiple systems.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/concurrent-versus-parallel-execution?u=76281980&t=160)** Since we've structured ourselves as concurrent operations, I can begin slicing cucumbers with this processor.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/concurrent-versus-parallel-execution?u=76281980&t=166)** - While I cut onions with the other one.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/concurrent-versus-parallel-execution?u=76281980&t=170)** Now we're actually executing in parallel because we're both executing at the same time, and as a result, we're able to finish the job faster.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/concurrent-versus-parallel-execution?u=76281980&t=180)** Concurrency is about the structure of a program, being able to deal with multiple things at once, whereas parallelism is about simultaneous execution, actually doing multiple things at once.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/concurrent-versus-parallel-execution?u=76281980&t=193)** Those things could be related like chopping vegetables, but they don't have to be.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/concurrent-versus-parallel-execution?u=76281980&t=198)** Concurrency enables a program to execute in parallel given the necessary hardware, but a concurrent program is not inherently parallel.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/concurrent-versus-parallel-execution?u=76281980&t=207)** - And programs may not always benefit from parallel execution.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/concurrent-versus-parallel-execution?u=76281980&t=210)** For example, the software drivers that handle I/O devices like a mouse, keyboard, and hard drive need to execute concurrently.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/concurrent-versus-parallel-execution?u=76281980&t=218)** They're managed by the operating system as independent things that get executed as needed.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/concurrent-versus-parallel-execution?u=76281980&t=223)** In a multi-core system, the execution of those drivers might get split amongst the available processors.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/concurrent-versus-parallel-execution?u=76281980&t=229)** However, since I/O operations occur rather infrequently relative to the speed at which computer operates, we don't really gain anything from parallel execution.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/concurrent-versus-parallel-execution?u=76281980&t=239)** Those sparse independent tasks could run just fine on a single processor and we wouldn't feel a difference.
>
> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/concurrent-versus-parallel-execution?u=76281980&t=246)** Concurrent programming is useful for I/O dependent tasks like graphical user interfaces.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/concurrent-versus-parallel-execution?u=76281980&t=252)** When the user clicks a button to execute an operation, that might take a while.
>
> **[4:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/concurrent-versus-parallel-execution?u=76281980&t=257)** To avoid locking up the user interface until it's completed, we can run the operation in a separate concurrent thread.
>
> **[4:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/concurrent-versus-parallel-execution?u=76281980&t=264)** This leaves the thread that's running the UI free to accept new inputs.
>
> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/concurrent-versus-parallel-execution?u=76281980&t=268)** - That sort of I/O dependent task is a good use case for concurrency.
>
> **[4:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/concurrent-versus-parallel-execution?u=76281980&t=273)** Parallel processing really becomes useful for computationally intensive tasks, such as calculating the result of multiplying two matrices together.
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/concurrent-versus-parallel-execution?u=76281980&t=282)** When large math operations can be divided into independent subparts, executing those parts in parallel on separate processors can really speed things up.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

> **Code Keywords:** let (1), while. (1), interface (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (2), refers to (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (2)
> **Analogies:** for example (1), such as (1)

#### Execution scheduling
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/execution-scheduling?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/execution-scheduling?u=76281980&t=0)** - Threads don't just execute whenever they want to.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/execution-scheduling?u=76281980&t=3)** A computer might have hundreds of processes with thousands of threads that all want their turn to run on just a handful of processors.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/execution-scheduling?u=76281980&t=11)** So how do they decide who goes first?
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/execution-scheduling?u=76281980&t=14)** - That's the operating system's job.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/execution-scheduling?u=76281980&t=16)** The OS includes a scheduler that controls when different threads and processes get their turn to execute on the CPU.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/execution-scheduling?u=76281980&t=24)** The scheduler makes it possible for multiple programs to run concurrently on a single processor.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/execution-scheduling?u=76281980&t=29)** When a process is created and ready to run, it gets loaded into memory and placed in the ready queue.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/execution-scheduling?u=76281980&t=36)** Think of these as cooks in the kitchen that are ready to work.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/execution-scheduling?u=76281980&t=39)** The scheduler is like the head chef that tells the other cooks when they get to use the cutting board.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/execution-scheduling?u=76281980&t=44)** It cycles through the ready processes so they get a chance to execute on the processor.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/execution-scheduling?u=76281980&t=50)** If there are multiple processors, then the OS will schedule processes to run on each of them to make the most use of the additional resources.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/execution-scheduling?u=76281980&t=58)** A process will run until it finishes, and then a scheduler will assign another process to execute on that processor.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/execution-scheduling?u=76281980&t=65)** Or a process might get blocked and have to wait for an I/O event, in which case, they'll go into a separate I/O waiting queue so another process can run.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/execution-scheduling?u=76281980&t=75)** Or the scheduler might determine that a process has spent its fair share of time on the processor and swap it out for another process from the ready queue.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/execution-scheduling?u=76281980&t=85)** When that occurs, it's called a context switch.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/execution-scheduling?u=76281980&t=88)** The operating system has to save the state or context of the process that was running, so it can be resumed later, and it has to load the context of the new process that's about to run.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/execution-scheduling?u=76281980&t=100)** If I'm a process that's executing on this processor to chop cucumbers, when a scheduler decides it's time for a context switch, I'll need to pause what I'm doing and store the state of that task.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/execution-scheduling?u=76281980&t=112)** - And as the new process that just got scheduled, I'll load my state information and then begin executing.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/execution-scheduling?u=76281980&t=119)** Now, context switches are not instantaneous.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/execution-scheduling?u=76281980&t=122)** It takes time to save and restore the registers in memory state, so the scheduler needs a strategy for how frequently it switches between processes.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/execution-scheduling?u=76281980&t=131)** There's a wide variety of algorithms that different operating system schedulers implement.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/execution-scheduling?u=76281980&t=136)** Some of these algorithms are preemptive, which means they may pause or preempt a running low-priority task when a higher priority task enters the ready state.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/execution-scheduling?u=76281980&t=147)** In non-preemptive algorithms, once a process enters the running state, it'll be allowed to run for its allotted time.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/execution-scheduling?u=76281980&t=154)** Which algorithm a scheduler chooses to implement will depend on its goals.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/execution-scheduling?u=76281980&t=158)** Some schedulers might try to maximize throughput or the amount of work they complete in a given time, whereas others might aim to minimize latency to improve the system's responsiveness.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/execution-scheduling?u=76281980&t=170)** Different operating systems have different purposes, and a desktop OS like windows will have a different set of goals and use a different type of scheduler than a real-time OS for embedded systems.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/execution-scheduling?u=76281980&t=182)** Now, while it's important to understand the concept of scheduling and that it's taking place, you usually don't need to worry about the nitty-gritty details of how the scheduler works because it's often handled under the hood by the operating system.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/execution-scheduling?u=76281980&t=196)** In fact, you might not have any control over when the parts of your program actually execute,
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/execution-scheduling?u=76281980&t=202)** - And that's an important thing to keep in mind.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/execution-scheduling?u=76281980&t=204)** Avoid writing programs expecting that multiple threads or processes will execute in a certain order or for an equal amount of time because the operating system may choose to schedule them differently from run to run.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Speakers:** - and (2), - threads (1), - that (1)
> **Code Keywords:** switch (2), case, (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (1)
> **Env Vars:** cpu (1)
> **Warnings:** keep in mind (1)

#### Execution scheduling: C++ demo

>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/execution-scheduling-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=13)** The vegetable_chopper function on line nine takes in a string argument for a name which can be used to uniquely identify this thread.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/execution-scheduling-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=21)** The function begins by initializing a local variable on line 10 to count the number of vegetables this thread chops.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/execution-scheduling-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=29)** The while loop on line 11 will execute as long as a Boolean chopping variable that we initialized on line seven is true.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/execution-scheduling-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=37)** And with each loop iteration, it increments the value of the local vegetable_count variable.

>

>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/execution-scheduling-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=67)** Now we're creating and starting these two threads one after the other.

> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/execution-scheduling-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=72)** So they won't begin at the exact same time, but they'll begin close enough together for the purpose of this demonstration.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/execution-scheduling-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=80)** After starting both threads, the program prints a message and then sleeps for one second before setting the chopping variable to false on line 23, which will stop the while loop in both threads.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/execution-scheduling-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=93)** Now I'll switch to a command prompt where I've already used the make command to compile the source file.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/execution-scheduling-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=100)** So I'll run the example, execution_scheduling_demo.exe.

> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/execution-scheduling-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=106)** I get an initial message that Barron and Olivia are chopping vegetables.

> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/execution-scheduling-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=109)** Then after a second, they both finish and print a message with the number of vegetables each thread chopped, which is quite a lot.

> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/execution-scheduling-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=124)** It looks like Olivia chopped more than Barron.

> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/execution-scheduling-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=127)** Scheduling is not always fair.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/execution-scheduling-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=130)** If I run this program again, now Barron and Olivia both end with different amounts than before.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/execution-scheduling-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=138)** As you can see, scheduling is not consistent from run to run, so your programs should not rely on execution scheduling for correctness.
