#### Thread life cycle
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-life-cycle?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-life-cycle?u=76281980&t=1)** - When a new process or program begins running, it will start with just one thread, which is called the main thread, because it's the main one that runs when the program begins.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-life-cycle?u=76281980&t=11)** That main thread can then start or spawn additional threads to help out, referred to as its child threads, which are part of the same process, but execute independently to do other tasks.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-life-cycle?u=76281980&t=23)** Those threads can spawn their own children if needed, and as each of those threads finish executing, they'll notify their parent and terminate with the main thread, usually being the last to finish execution.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-life-cycle?u=76281980&t=36)** Over the life cycle of a thread, from creation through execution, and finally, termination, threads will usually be in one of four states.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-life-cycle?u=76281980&t=45)** If I'm the main thread in this kitchen and I spawn or create another thread to help me, that child thread will begin in the new state.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-life-cycle?u=76281980&t=53)** - Hello.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-life-cycle?u=76281980&t=55)** - This thread isn't actually running yet, so it doesn't take any CPU resources.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-life-cycle?u=76281980&t=59)** - I don't even know what I'm supposed to be doing.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-life-cycle?u=76281980&t=62)** - Part of creating a new thread is assigning it a function, the code it's going to execute.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-life-cycle?u=76281980&t=67)** Olivia, I need you to slice these sausages, we're making soup. - I can do that.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-life-cycle?u=76281980&t=76)** I'm ready to start.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-life-cycle?u=76281980&t=77)** - Right. You can start now.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-life-cycle?u=76281980&t=80)** Some programming languages require you to explicitly start a thread after creating it.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-life-cycle?u=76281980&t=86)** - [Olivia] Now that I've started, I'm in the runnable state, which means the operating system can schedule me to execute.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-life-cycle?u=76281980&t=93)** Through contact switches, I'll get swapped out with other threads to run on one of the available processors.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-life-cycle?u=76281980&t=99)** - Olivia is running independently now, so my thread is free to continue executing my own tasks when it's my turn to get scheduled on the processor.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-life-cycle?u=76281980&t=108)** - Ah, man, this sausage is frozen.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-life-cycle?u=76281980&t=111)** I need to wait for it to thaw before I can continue.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-life-cycle?u=76281980&t=115)** When a thread needs to wait for an event to occur, like an external input or a timer, it goes into a blocked state while it waits.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-life-cycle?u=76281980&t=123)** The good thing is that while I'm blocked, I'm not using any CPU resources.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-life-cycle?u=76281980&t=128)** The operating system will return me to the runnable state when the sausage is thawed.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-life-cycle?u=76281980&t=133)** - And that frees up the processor for other threads to use.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-life-cycle?u=76281980&t=136)** Now, my thread may eventually reach a point where I need to wait until one of my children threads has finished for me to continue on.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-life-cycle?u=76281980&t=144)** Maybe I've finished preparing everything else, I've completed all of my tasks, and I need Olivia to finish slicing the sausage.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-life-cycle?u=76281980&t=151)** I can wait for her thread to complete its execution by calling the join method.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-life-cycle?u=76281980&t=157)** When I call join, my thread will enter a blocked state waiting until Olivia's done.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-life-cycle?u=76281980&t=162)** - Ah, the sausage is finally thawed.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-life-cycle?u=76281980&t=165)** Now I'll go back to the runnable state and continue executing.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-life-cycle?u=76281980&t=169)** (video tape whirring) Now I've finished executing, so I'll notify my parent thread that I'm done.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-life-cycle?u=76281980&t=178)** Hey, Baron! I'm done.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-life-cycle?u=76281980&t=180)** And then I'll enter the final terminated state. (sighs) A thread enters the terminated state when it either completes its execution or is abnormally aborted.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-life-cycle?u=76281980&t=193)** - Since Olivia notified me that she's done, I'll return to the runnable state so I can continue preparing soup.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-life-cycle?u=76281980&t=200)** Now, different programming languages may use different names for those states and have a few additional ones, but in general, new, runnable, blocked, and terminated are the four phases of the life cycle of a thread.

> **Env Vars:** cpu (2)

>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-life-cycle-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=161)** While C++ doesn't give us a view into a thread state in regard to whether it's blocked or running in the operating system, we can check whether or not a thread is still considered active by using the joinable function, which returns a Boolean indicating whether or not that thread is alive.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-life-cycle-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=179)** I'll add a print statement immediately after creating Chef Olivia's thread on line 15 to show whether or not her thread is still active.

#### Detached thread
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/detached-thread?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/detached-thread?u=76281980&t=1)** - We often create threads to provide some sort of service or perform a periodic task in support of the main program.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/detached-thread?u=76281980&t=8)** A common example of that is garbage collection.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/detached-thread?u=76281980&t=12)** A garbage collector is a form of automatic memory management that runs in the background and attempts to reclaim garbage or memory that's no longer being used by the program.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/detached-thread?u=76281980&t=22)** Many languages include garbage collection as a standard part of their runtime environment.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/detached-thread?u=76281980&t=27)** But for this demonstration, I'll spawn my own new thread to handle garbage collection.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/detached-thread?u=76281980&t=33)** - Man, what a mess.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/detached-thread?u=76281980&t=35)** - Olivia is a separate child thread that will execute independently of my main thread so I can continue doing what I'm doing here, getting my soup ingredients ready.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/detached-thread?u=76281980&t=45)** - Well, I try to reclaim some memory, or counter space, by clearing up Baron's garbage.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/detached-thread?u=76281980&t=50)** - This setup with Olivia running as a separate thread to provide that garbage collection service will work fine until I'm ready to finish executing.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/detached-thread?u=76281980&t=60)** Bam, now my soup spiced and ready.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/detached-thread?u=76281980&t=63)** My main thread is done executing and I'm ready to exit the program, but I can't.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/detached-thread?u=76281980&t=69)** - Because I'm still running.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/detached-thread?u=76281980&t=71)** Since Baron's spawned me as a normal child thread, he won't be able to exit until I've terminated.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/detached-thread?u=76281980&t=77)** - And since Olivia's thread is designed to collect garbage in a continuous loop, she'll never exit.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/detached-thread?u=76281980&t=82)** I'll be stuck here waiting forever, and this process will never terminate.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/detached-thread?u=76281980&t=86)** Threads that are performing background tasks, like garbage collection, can be detached from the main program by making them what's called a demon thread.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/detached-thread?u=76281980&t=95)** A Daemon thread, which you may also hear pronounced as Daemon, is a thread that will not prevent the program from exiting if it's still running.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/detached-thread?u=76281980&t=103)** By default, new threads are usually spawned as non-Daemon or normal threads, and you have to explicitly turn a thread into a Daemon or background thread.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/detached-thread?u=76281980&t=113)** Olivia, I forgot to tell you this earlier, but you are a Daemon thread.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/detached-thread?u=76281980&t=120)** - Oh man, you detached me.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/detached-thread?u=76281980&t=122)** - When my main thread is finished executing and there aren't any non-Daemon threads left running, this process can terminate and Olivia's Daemon thread will terminate with it.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/detached-thread?u=76281980&t=134)** - Since I was terminated abruptly with the process, I didn't have a chance to gracefully shut down and stop what I was doing.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/detached-thread?u=76281980&t=141)** That's fine in the case of a garbage collection routine, because all of the memory this process was using will get cleared as part of terminating it.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/detached-thread?u=76281980&t=149)** But if I was doing some sort of IO operation, like writing to a file, then terminating in the middle of that operation could end up corrupting data.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/detached-thread?u=76281980&t=159)** If you detach a thread to make it a background task, make sure it won't have any negative side effects if it prematurely exits.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Speakers:** - we (1), - man (1), - olivia (1), - well (1), - this (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (3)
> **CLI Commands:** make (2)
> **Code Keywords:** continue (1), default, (1)
> **Prerequisites:** setup (1)

#### Detached thread: C++ demo
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/detached-thread-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/detached-thread-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In this program, to demonstrate detaching a thread, we defined a function called kitchen_cleaner on line 7, which represents a periodic background task like garbage collection.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/detached-thread-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=11)** The kitchen_cleaner uses an infinite wild loop to continuously print a message that Olivia cleaned the kitchen once every second.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/detached-thread-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=19)** Down in the program's main function, I start a new kitchen_cleaner thread named olivia on line 15, then the thread prints a series of three messages that Barron is cooking, followed by a message that Barron is done on line 20 before finally waiting for Olivia's thread to finish and join.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/detached-thread-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=40)** If I run the program, I see those messages from Barron and Olivia displayed, but after the main thread reaches the end and prints its final Barron is done message, the program doesn't exit because the kitchen_cleaner thread is still going strong, and it will continue to run forever, so I'll terminate it by pressing Control and the Break key.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/detached-thread-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=65)** Now, to prevent that from happening, I'll detach the thread named olivia by calling the detach function after creating it on line 15.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/detached-thread-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=85)** This will allow the thread to run independently in the background.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/detached-thread-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=89)** It also makes the thread non-joinable, so I can delete the join function on line 22.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/detached-thread-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=103)** Now when I make and run the program again, when the main thread is done executing, Olivia's thread is also terminated, so the process can exit.

### 3. Mutual Exclusion

#### Data race
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/data-race?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/data-race?u=76281980&t=1)** - One of the main challenges of writing concurrent programs is identifying the possible dependencies between threads to make sure they don't interfere with each other and cause problems.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/data-race?u=76281980&t=11)** Data races are a common problem that can occur when two or more threads are concurrently accessing the same location in memory, and at least one of those threads is writing to that location to modify its value.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/data-race?u=76281980&t=25)** Fortunately, you can protect your program against data races by using synchronization techniques, which we'll show you later.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/data-race?u=76281980&t=32)** But to eventually use those techniques, you'll first need to know how to recognize the data race.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/data-race?u=76281980&t=38)** Olivia and I are two concurrent threads working together to figure out what we need to buy from the grocery store.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/data-race?u=76281980&t=45)** I'll take inventory of the pantry, and when I see that we're running low on something, I'll add more of that item to our shared shopping list.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/data-race?u=76281980&t=52)** - And while Baron does that, I'll look through my recipe book and I'll add ingredients to our shopping list for the meals I want us to cook this week.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/data-race?u=76281980&t=60)** - Even though we're two separate threads doing different tasks, we run the risk of a data race because we're both accessing and modifying the same shared resource, our shopping list.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/data-race?u=76281980&t=71)** Now to the pantry.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/data-race?u=76281980&t=73)** - Oh, this garlic mashed potato recipe looks delicious.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/data-race?u=76281980&t=77)** I'll need five potatoes for it.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/data-race?u=76281980&t=80)** I see that our shopping list already has three potatoes on it.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/data-race?u=76281980&t=83)** Three plus five is eight, so I'll erase three.

>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/data-race?u=76281980&t=95)** As you can see, even a simple operation, like adding two numbers, is actually a multiple step process.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/data-race?u=76281980&t=102)** First, I had to read the existing value of that item from the shopping list.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/data-race?u=76281980&t=107)** Then I modified the value by adding what I needed to it, and finally, I wrote the result back to the shopping list.

>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/data-race?u=76281980&t=128)** One plus two is uh, uh.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/data-race?u=76281980&t=132)** - My garlic mashed potato recipe calls for five cloves of garlic.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/data-race?u=76281980&t=136)** I see there's currently one clove of garlic on the list.

>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/data-race?u=76281980&t=157)** - And now we have a problem.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/data-race?u=76281980&t=159)** The shopping list started with one clove of garlic.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/data-race?u=76281980&t=162)** Baron wanted to add two more, and I need to add five more.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/data-race?u=76281980&t=166)** One plus two plus five means we should have ended up with eight cloves of garlic on this list, but somehow we only have three.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/data-race?u=76281980&t=174)** I need more garlic for my mashed potatoes.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/data-race?u=76281980&t=176)** - We just had a data race.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/data-race?u=76281980&t=178)** As concurrent threads, it's up to the operating system to schedule when we each get to execute.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/data-race?u=76281980&t=184)** Right after I read the value of one from that shared shopping list, my thread got paused.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/data-race?u=76281980&t=189)** - Then my thread became active and changed the number of garlic from one to six.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/data-race?u=76281980&t=194)** - And finally, my thread became active again.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/data-race?u=76281980&t=197)** And at that point I was operating with old data in my local memory because I thought the existing value of garlic on the shopping list was still one.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/data-race?u=76281980&t=205)** So I finished my operation by changing it to three.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/data-race?u=76281980&t=208)** In this example, it was the unfortunate timing of when our threads were scheduled that caused the problem.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/data-race?u=76281980&t=214)** But the unpredictability of when threads get scheduled means sometimes the data race will occur and cause problems, but other times everything might work just fine.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/data-race?u=76281980&t=224)** And that inconsistency makes data races a real challenge to recognize and debug.

> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/data-race-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=9)** The variable on line six is a counter for the amount of garlic we should buy and gets initialized to zero.

>

>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/data-race-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=109)** And if I run the program again, I get a different output.
>

>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/data-race-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=124)** My two concurrent threads end up stepping on each other's toes and unintentionally overriding each other's changes to produce an incorrect result.
>

>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/data-race-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=140)** There are tools that exist to help detect data races, but they're specific to different languages and environments and beyond the scope of this course.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/data-race-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=149)** Some languages also have compiler flags that you can implement to point out sections of code that had the potential to cause a data race.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/data-race-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=157)** In my opinion, the best defense against data races is a strong offense, preventing them from occurring in the first place.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/data-race-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=164)** Since a data race only occurs when at least one of the concurrent threads is modifying the value of a memory location, pay close attention to anywhere you see an assignment operation or an operator like the ++ increment that changes a variable's value.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/data-race-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=180)** If there's a potential that two or more threads could access that variable, then you will almost certainly need to use some mechanism to protect it.

#### Mutual exclusion
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mutual-exclusion?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mutual-exclusion?u=76281980&t=1)** - Anytime multiple threads are concurrently reading and writing a shared resource, it creates the potential for incorrect behavior like a data race.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mutual-exclusion?u=76281980&t=9)** But we can defend against that by identifying and protecting critical sections of code.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mutual-exclusion?u=76281980&t=15)** A critical section or critical region is part of a program that accesses a shared resource, such as a data structure in memory or an external device, and it may not operate correctly if multiple threads concurrently access it.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mutual-exclusion?u=76281980&t=29)** The critical section needs to be protected so that it only allows one thread or process to execute in it at a time.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mutual-exclusion?u=76281980&t=36)** - Baron and I experienced a data race as we added garlic to our shared shopping list because incrementing a value is actually a three-step process.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mutual-exclusion?u=76281980&t=46)** Read the current value, modify it, and then write back the result.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mutual-exclusion?u=76281980&t=52)** Those three steps are a critical section, and they need to execute as an uninterrupted action so we don't accidentally override each other.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mutual-exclusion?u=76281980&t=60)** - I have an idea. Give me your pencil.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mutual-exclusion?u=76281980&t=64)** - Hey, I was using that.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mutual-exclusion?u=76281980&t=67)** - Now there's only one pencil for us to share, and the rule is that only the person holding the pencil can access the shopping list, either to read or write it.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mutual-exclusion?u=76281980&t=76)** That way one of us won't accidentally read a wrong value because the other one is only halfway done updating it.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mutual-exclusion?u=76281980&t=83)** In this arrangement, the pencil is serving as a mechanism called a mutex, short for mutual exclusion, which you'll also hear referred to as a lock.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mutual-exclusion?u=76281980&t=92)** Only one thread or process can have possession of the lock at a time, so it can be used to prevent multiple threads from simultaneously accessing a shared resource, forcing them to take turns.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mutual-exclusion?u=76281980&t=103)** If either of us wants to access the shopping list, we first need to pick up the pencil to acquire the lock on it.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mutual-exclusion?u=76281980&t=110)** We do whatever we need to with the shared notepad, and then when we're done, release the lock by putting down the pencil.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mutual-exclusion?u=76281980&t=117)** The operation to acquire the lock is an atomic operation, which means it's always executed as a single, indivisible action.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mutual-exclusion?u=76281980&t=126)** To the rest of the system, an atomic operation appears to happen instantaneously, even if under the hood, it really takes multiple steps.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mutual-exclusion?u=76281980&t=135)** The key here is that the atomic operation is uninterruptible.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mutual-exclusion?u=76281980&t=139)** - So if I grab the pencil.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mutual-exclusion?u=76281980&t=141)** - Acquiring the mutex is an atomic action that no other thread can interfere with halfway through.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mutual-exclusion?u=76281980&t=147)** Either you have the mutex or you don't.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mutual-exclusion?u=76281980&t=150)** And now that you do have a lock on our pencil, you can safely execute in the critical section.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mutual-exclusion?u=76281980&t=155)** - I see we already have 10 carrots on the list.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mutual-exclusion?u=76281980&t=158)** I'll add five more to that.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mutual-exclusion?u=76281980&t=159)** - Oh, and we're going to need some more onions too.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mutual-exclusion?u=76281980&t=162)** - Well, I currently possess the mutex, so you'll have to wait until I'm done.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mutual-exclusion?u=76281980&t=167)** - Threads that try to acquire a lock that's currently possessed by another thread can pause and wait till it's available.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mutual-exclusion?u=76281980&t=174)** There, I'm done with the notepad for now, so I'll release the lock.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mutual-exclusion?u=76281980&t=179)** - And I'll acquire the lock so I can add onions to the list.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mutual-exclusion?u=76281980&t=187)** - Don't forget to release the mutex when you're done.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mutual-exclusion?u=76281980&t=190)** - Okay.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mutual-exclusion?u=76281980&t=192)** - Since threads can get blocked and stuck waiting for a thread in the critical section to finish executing, it's important to keep the section of code protected with the mutex as short as possible.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mutual-exclusion?u=76281980&t=203)** If I take the pencil, execute a critical section by adding more lettuce, and then hold onto the pencil while I contemplate what else to buy.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mutual-exclusion?u=76281980&t=213)** - I'm stuck waiting for Olivia to return the pencil so I can use it, and getting kind of annoyed.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mutual-exclusion?u=76281980&t=219)** - Only thinking about what I want to buy doesn't actually require the shared notepad, so the operation doesn't require mutual exclusion.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mutual-exclusion?u=76281980&t=227)** I should have returned the pencil as soon as I was done updating the list.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mutual-exclusion?u=76281980&t=231)** That way, Baron could use it to execute the critical section while I'm busy doing other things.

#### Atomic objects: C++ demo
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/atomic-objects-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/atomic-objects-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Using a lock to protect a shared variable with mutual exclusion works, but if you're only doing simple operations, like incrementing a variable's value, then a simpler solution is to use C++ atomic types, which encapsulate a value and synchronize access to it to prevent a data race.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/atomic-objects-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=19)** If I scroll down this documentation page, I see a variety of atomic types, including atomic booleans, characters, integers, long values, and so on.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/atomic-objects-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=31)** Continuing down the page is a list of functions of the atomic object, which implement many of the common operations.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/atomic-objects-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=39)** To demonstrate using atomic objects, I'm going to modify the previous example code to demonstrate a data race, which has two parallel threads incrementing a count variable 10 million times.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/atomic-objects-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=51)** I should get an output of 20 million, but since I'm using a regular integer on line 6 and I don't have any locks in place to protect it with mutual exclusion, when I run this program, it has a data race that produces an incorrect result, less than the expected output of 20 million.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/atomic-objects-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=69)** To use an atomic object, I must first include the atomic header.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/atomic-objects-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=80)** Next, I'll replace the garlic_count variable on line 7 with an atomic unsigned int and I'll initialize it to a value of zero.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/atomic-objects-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=103)** Finally, in the printf section on line 20, I'll use the atomic objects load function to read its stored value.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/atomic-objects-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=122)** Now, when I make and run the program, I get the correct output value of 20 million because each of the increment operations was executed atomically.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/atomic-objects-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=131)** Keep atomic objects in mind whenever you need to share something simple, like a counter value between concurrent threads.

### 4. Locks

#### Recursive mutex
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex?u=76281980&t=1)** - Olivia and I have been using this pencil as a mutex.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex?u=76281980&t=4)** Only one person at a time can own or have a lock on it, and only that person can access our shared resource: this notepad.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex?u=76281980&t=12)** - If I attempt to lock the mutex while another thread has it, my thread will be blocked and I need to wait until he unlocks it so it becomes available.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex?u=76281980&t=20)** - And if I attempt to lock the mutex, it doesn't appear to be available, so my thread will just have to wait too.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex?u=76281980&t=28)** - It's behind your ear. You already locked it.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex?u=76281980&t=31)** - Oh.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex?u=76281980&t=32)** Well, my thread can't unlock the mutex while I'm blocked waiting on it, and I'll be waiting on the mutex forever because I'll never be able to unlock it.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex?u=76281980&t=42)** I'm stuck and so are you.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex?u=76281980&t=45)** If a thread tries to lock a mutex that it's already locked, it'll enter into a waiting list for that mutex which results in something called a deadlock because no other thread can unlock that mutex.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex?u=76281980&t=56)** - There may be times when a program needs to lock a mutex multiple times before unlocking it.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex?u=76281980&t=62)** In that case, you should use a reentrant mutex to prevent this type of problem.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex?u=76281980&t=66)** A reentrant mutex is a particular type of mutex that can be locked multiple times by the same process or thread.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex?u=76281980&t=74)** Internally, the reentrant mutex keeps track of how many times it's been locked by the owning thread, and it has to be unlocked an equal number of times before another thread can lock it.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex?u=76281980&t=85)** If this pencil is a reentrant mutex, when I pick it up, I lock it.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex?u=76281980&t=90)** - Now Olivia's thread has a hold on the mutex, so she's the only one that can lock or unlock it.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex?u=76281980&t=95)** - Since the pencil is reentrant, I can lock it again.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex?u=76281980&t=98)** Now the pencil has been locked twice by me, which means I'll have to unlock it twice to fully release my hold on it.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex?u=76281980&t=105)** If your program needs to lock a mutex multiple times, using a reentrant mutex may seem like an easy way to avoid a deadlock, but if you don't unlock the reentrant mutex the same number of times, you can still end up stuck.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex?u=76281980&t=119)** - I'm waiting.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex?u=76281980&t=122)** Thanks.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex?u=76281980&t=123)** Many programmers like using reentrant locks because it can make things easier.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex?u=76281980&t=128)** You don't need to worry as much about what's already been locked, and they make it easier to retrofit locks into existing code.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex?u=76281980&t=134)** As an example, say I have a function to increment a shared counter and it uses a mutex to protect that operation.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex?u=76281980&t=141)** If later I create another function that uses the same mutex to protect some other section of code and that section of code uses the increment counter function, since those functions are nested, when I execute my function, it'll end up locking the mutex twice before unlocking it.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex?u=76281980&t=159)** If I was using a regular non-reentrant lock, that would produce a deadlock, but with a reentrant mutex, this works just fine.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex?u=76281980&t=168)** Now, like many things in the world of programmers, there are some very strong opinions about whether reentrant locks are good or evil.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex?u=76281980&t=175)** Some opponents of using reentrant locks will argue that the example I just showed you should be refactored to avoid having nested locks by using a third function that increments the counter and only gets called from within a protected section.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex?u=76281980&t=190)** I'm not going to advocate either way on this debate.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex?u=76281980&t=192)** There are pros and cons to both sides.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex?u=76281980&t=195)** - One use case where reentrant locks are really needed is when writing a recursive function; that is a function that calls itself.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex?u=76281980&t=203)** If the function makes a recursive call to itself from within a locked section, it will lock the mutex multiple times as it repeats itself, and then unlock the mutex an equal number of times as it returns and unwinds.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex?u=76281980&t=218)** Since a reentrant mutex can be used recursively like this, you'll often hear it referred to as a recursive mutex or a recursive lock.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex?u=76281980&t=227)** Different languages use different terms, but these all basically mean the same thing.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** function (8), case, (1), protected (1), this, (1)
> **Speakers:** - olivia (1), - if (1), - and (1), - it (1), - oh (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (3)
> **CLI Commands:** make (2)

#### Recursive mutex: C++ demo
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] To demonstrate using a recursive mutex with C++, we've modified the previous example that we used to demonstrate a data race and mutual exclusion with two shoppers that concurrently increment the number of items to buy.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=13)** In this version, we're counting the amount of garlic and potatoes to buy with the variables that are initialized on lines seven and eight.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=22)** There are two helper functions on lines 11 and 17 called add_garlic and add_potato, which increment the corresponding garlic_count or potato_count variables.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=33)** And each of those functions lock and unlock the same mutex named pencil to enforce mutual exclusion around those operations and prevent a data race.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=43)** The shopper function simply uses a for loop on line 24 to execute those add_garlic and add_potato functions 10,000 times each.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=52)** Down in the program's main function, we create and start two shopper threads.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=56)** Then after they finish running, we print out the amount of garlic and potatoes to buy.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=63)** Running this program as is indicates we need 20,000 garlic and 20,000 potatoes.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=69)** That's quite a feast.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=72)** Now, let's say every time we add a potato, we also want to add additional garlic because potatoes and garlic go really well together.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=81)** To do that, we might add a call to the add_garlic function inside of the add_potato function, immediately after incrementing the potato_count.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=97)** By inserting the add_garlic function, we've effectively nested two calls to the lock function.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=103)** When the add_potato function gets called, it will lock the pencil.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=107)** Then when the add_potato function calls add_garlic, the add_garlic function will lock the pencil again.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=114)** So the pencil ends up being locked twice in a row by those nested functions before then being unlocked twice as the functions complete.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=124)** When I make and run this program, it gets stuck waiting when it tries to lock the pencil a second time.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=131)** The program doesn't have any output, so I can't really tell what's going on, but if I try to wait for it to finish, I'll be waiting here forever.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=140)** So I'll press Ctrl and the break key to end it.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=144)** Fortunately, the fix is simple.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=147)** All I need to do is change the pencil declaration on line nine from being a regular mutex to a recursive mutex, which can be locked multiple times before being unlocked.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=164)** Now when I make and then run the program, I get the output that we should buy 40,000 garlic and 20,000 potatoes.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/recursive-mutex-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=174)** That makes sense because I increment garlic_count in both the add_garlic and add_potato functions.

#### Try lock
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/try-lock?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/try-lock?u=76281980&t=1)** - When multiple threads each have multiple tasks to perform, making those threads block and wait every time they attempt to acquire a lock that's already taken may not be necessary or efficient.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/try-lock?u=76281980&t=12)** Olivia and I are two threads doing several different tasks.

> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/try-lock?u=76281980&t=16)** My thread will be taking an inventory of the fridge to see what things we're running low on, and then add those to the shopping list on our shared notepad.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/try-lock?u=76281980&t=25)** I'll go back and forth between those two tasks,

> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/try-lock?u=76281980&t=54)** If I attempt to lock a mutex in a regular blocking fashion, my thread would enter a waiting state at this point, doing nothing until Olivia unlocks it.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/try-lock?u=76281980&t=63)** If I don't have anything else to do, so I can't continue with other things until after I've accessed the shared notepad, that's okay; it's what has to happen.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/try-lock?u=76281980&t=72)** But in this scenario, I do have other useful things to do that don't require the notepad.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/try-lock?u=76281980&t=77)** I can keep searching the fridge for other things we need.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/try-lock?u=76281980&t=80)** So rather than using the standard locking method to acquire the mutex, I'll use what's called try lock or try enter, which is a non-blocking version of the lock or acquire method.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/try-lock?u=76281980&t=92)** It returns immediately and one of two things will happen.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/try-lock?u=76281980&t=95)** If the mutex you're trying to lock is available, it will get locked and the method will return true.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/try-lock?u=76281980&t=102)** Otherwise, if the mutex is already possessed by another thread, the try lock method will immediately return false.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/try-lock?u=76281980&t=109)** That return value of true or false lets the thread know whether or not it was successful in acquiring the lock.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/try-lock?u=76281980&t=117)** So if I try to lock the pencil that Olivia currently has, I know immediately that my attempt has failed, so I can go back to searching the fridge
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/try-lock?u=76281980&t=127)** - There.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/try-lock?u=76281980&t=128)** I'm done writing for now, so I'll unlock the pencil and go back to searching for coupons.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/try-lock?u=76281980&t=133)** Since Barron wasn't blocked waiting for this mutex, it's just sitting unlocked, available for anyone to take.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/try-lock?u=76281980&t=139)** Now, Barron likes to explain try lock with pencils and notepads.

> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/try-lock?u=76281980&t=144)** I think of it more like being at a house party with a bunch of your friends, your fellow threads.

> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/try-lock?u=76281980&t=149)** There's one restroom at the house that everyone at the party will need to use at some point, but only one person can use it at a time.

#### Try lock: C++ demo

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/try-lock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] To demonstrate using try_lock in C++, we created this example which simulates two shoppers searching for items they need and then adding them to a shared notepad.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/try-lock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=11)** The variable on line eight represents the number of items on the notepad.

>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/try-lock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=31)** The while loop on line 13 will keep the shoppers searching for items and adding them to the notepad until there are at least 20 items.

> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/try-lock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=39)** If the shopper has items to add to the notepad, they'll execute the if clause on line 14 in which they lock the pencil mutex, add all their items to the list, and print a message with how many items they've added.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/try-lock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=54)** That then resets their items_to_add count back to zero.
>

>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/try-lock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=88)** Then record the elapsed time from right before starting their threads until they both finish to see how long it takes them to find at least 20 items.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/try-lock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=98)** Switching over to the command prompt, I'll run the program.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/try-lock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=102)** Notice that the two threads are never adding more than one item at a time.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/try-lock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=107)** Since the lock function blocks execution while waiting for the mutex to be available, these two threads end up taking turns, swapping places between the top and bottom sections of the if-else statement.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/try-lock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=120)** Now that the program has finished executing, I see that it took them a little under 6.5 seconds.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/try-lock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=127)** Now to modify this code to use the try_lock function, I'll delete the regular lock on line 15.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/try-lock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=137)** Then I'll add a logical And operator to the if statement on line 14, followed by pencil.trylock.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/try-lock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=152)** The order of these statements on each side of the operator matters because C++ evaluates the left side of a logical And before the right side.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/try-lock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=163)** It will first check to see if there are items to add.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/try-lock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=166)** If and only if the left side of the And is true will it evaluate the right side and execute the try_lock function.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/try-lock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=174)** If the mutex is available, then calling try_lock will immediately lock it and return true, and the program will execute the code on lines 15 through 19 to add items to the shared notepad.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/try-lock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=187)** If the mutex was not available, then the try_lock function will immediately return false, and that thread will execute the else clause on lines 21 to 23 to look for other things to buy.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/try-lock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=208)** When I make and run the program now, it executes much faster, finishing in a little over two seconds, which is less than half the amount of time it took before.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/try-lock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=218)** Notice that this time, when one of the threads gets its turn in the critical section, it often adds more than one item to the notepad.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/try-lock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=226)** With the try_lock function in place, as one thread takes its time writing to the notepad, the other thread is able to jump past that section of code to search for other things to buy multiple times.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/try-lock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=238)** It's been freed up to go accomplish other things.

#### Shared mutex
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-mutex?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-mutex?u=76281980&t=1)** - We use a lock or mutex to protect a critical section of code to defend against data races, which can occur when multiple threads are concurrently accessing the same location in memory, and at least one of those threads is writing to that location.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-mutex?u=76281980&t=15)** That second part is key because if we have a bunch of threads and none of them are writing, they all just want to read from the same location, that's fine.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-mutex?u=76281980&t=24)** It's okay to let multiple threads read the same shared value as long as no one else can change it.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-mutex?u=76281980&t=30)** They'll all safely see the same thing.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-mutex?u=76281980&t=32)** Danger only exists when you add a thread that's writing to the mix.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-mutex?u=76281980&t=37)** When we use a basic lock or mutex to protect the shared resource, we limit access so that only one of the threads can use it at a time, regardless of whether that thread is reading or writing, or both.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-mutex?u=76281980&t=50)** - That works, but it's not necessarily the most efficient way to do things, especially when there are lots of threads that only need to read.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-mutex?u=76281980&t=59)** This is where reader-writer locks can be useful.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-mutex?u=76281980&t=62)** A reader-writer lock, or shared mutex, can be locked in one of two ways.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-mutex?u=76281980&t=68)** It can be locked in a shared read mode that allows multiple threads that only need to read simultaneously to lock it, or it can be locked in an exclusive write mode that limits access to only one thread at a time, allowing that thread to safely write to the shared resource.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-mutex?u=76281980&t=87)** A read-write lock is useful for protecting a shared resource like our calendar because Barron and I frequently need to read the calendar throughout the day.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-mutex?u=76281980&t=97)** - I can never remember what day it is.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-mutex?u=76281980&t=99)** - But we rarely need to modify it.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-mutex?u=76281980&t=101)** - Only once a day.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-mutex?u=76281980&t=103)** - This marker represents our shared mutex.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-mutex?u=76281980&t=106)** When my thread wants to read the calendar, I'll lock the mutex in the read-only mode by placing my finger on it.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-mutex?u=76281980&t=112)** - I also want to read the calendar.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-mutex?u=76281980&t=114)** I can also place my finger on the marker.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-mutex?u=76281980&t=117)** Now we both have a shared lock on it, so we can both concurrently read it.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-mutex?u=76281980&t=122)** - When I'm done checking the date, I'll release my lock on the mutex.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-mutex?u=76281980&t=126)** Now I think it's time to increment the calendar's date.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-mutex?u=76281980&t=130)** In other words, I want to modify it, and to do that, I'll need to lock the shared mutex in the exclusive write mode by picking it up.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-mutex?u=76281980&t=138)** - But I'm still holding onto the lock to read.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-mutex?u=76281980&t=142)** - A thread trying to acquire the lock in write mode can't do so as long as it's still being held by any other threads in the read mode, so I'll have to wait.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-mutex?u=76281980&t=153)** - Done.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-mutex?u=76281980&t=154)** - Now the shared mutex is completely free, so I'll pick it up to place a write lock on it and update the calendar.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-mutex?u=76281980&t=161)** - Oh, I already forgot what day it is, and I can't read the calendar now because Olivia has an exclusive hold on the lock to write.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-mutex?u=76281980&t=170)** Since only one thread can have the write lock at a time, all other threads wanting to read or write will have to wait until the lock becomes available again.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-mutex?u=76281980&t=179)** - Now, recognizing when to use a read-write lock is just as important as knowing how to use it.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-mutex?u=76281980&t=186)** In certain scenarios, read-write locks can improve a program's performance versus using a standard mutex, but they are more complicated to implement and they typically use more resources under the hood to keep track of the number of readers.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-mutex?u=76281980&t=201)** - And there can be language dependent differences in how they're implemented that affect performance.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-mutex?u=76281980&t=207)** Do they give preference to readers or writers that are trying to acquire the lock?
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-mutex?u=76281980&t=211)** Deciding which type of mutex to use is a complicated decision, but as a general rule of thumb, it makes sense to use a shared reader-writer lock when you have a lot more threads that will be reading from the shared data than the number of threads that will be writing to it, such as certain types of database applications.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-mutex?u=76281980&t=230)** If the majority of your threads are writing, then there's not much, if any, advantage to using a read-write lock.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Speakers:** - i (2), - but (2), - now (2), - we (1), - that (1)
> **Definitions:** in other words (1), is a  (1)
> **Code Keywords:** let (1)
> **Analogies:** such as (1)
> **Best Practices:** general rule (1)

#### Shared mutex: C++ demo
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-mutex-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980)
