>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-mutex-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=19)** To demonstrate using a shared mutex, this example program creates 10 threads to concurrently read what day it is from a shared calendar while two other threads update it.

>

>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-mutex-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=123)** Only one thread can ever be reading or writing at a time.

> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/shared-mutex-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=146)** The first thing I'll need to do is include the shared mutex header at the top of the program.

### 5. Liveness

#### Deadlock
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock?u=76281980&t=1)** - Olivia and I decided to take a snack break to demonstrate some of the problems that can occur when using locks.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock?u=76281980&t=7)** A classic example that's used to illustrate synchronization issues when multiple threads are competing for multiple locks is the dining philosopher's problem.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock?u=76281980&t=16)** In this scenario, Olivia and I are two philosophers or threads, doing what philosophers do best, thinking and eating.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock?u=76281980&t=25)** We both need to access a shared resource, this plate of sushi, and each time one of us takes a piece of sushi, we're modifying its value.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock?u=76281980&t=33)** The number of pieces that are left.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock?u=76281980&t=35)** The act of taking sushi from the plate is a critical section, so to protect it, we've devised a mutual exclusion process using these two chopsticks as mutexes.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock?u=76281980&t=46)** When I want to take a bite of sushi, I'll first pick up the chopstick closest to me to acquire a lock on it.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock?u=76281980&t=52)** Then I pick up the farther chopstick.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock?u=76281980&t=54)** Now I have possession of both locks.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock?u=76281980&t=57)** I'm in the critical section, so I'll take a piece of sushi and then put down the far chopstick to release my lock on it, and then the close chopstick.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock?u=76281980&t=68)** And finally, since I'm a philosopher, I'll go back to philosophizing.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock?u=76281980&t=73)** - Ah, eureka.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock?u=76281980&t=75)** That was an interesting thought.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock?u=76281980&t=76)** Well, I'm feeling hungry now, so I'll acquire the chopstick closest to me, and then the one further away, I'll take a piece of sushi and then release the far chopstick, and then the one closer to me.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock?u=76281980&t=93)** As dining philosophers we'll both continue to alternate between eating and thinking, but since we're operating as concurrent threads, neither one of us knows when the other one wants to eat or think, and that can lead to problems.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock?u=76281980&t=107)** If I get hungry again and pick up the chopstick closest to me.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock?u=76281980&t=110)** - And I also get hungry and pick up my close chopstick.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock?u=76281980&t=116)** I see we've come to an impasse.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock?u=76281980&t=118)** We've both acquired one of the two locks that we need, so we're both stuck waiting on the other thread to release the other lock to make progress.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock?u=76281980&t=126)** This is one example of a situation called deadlock.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock?u=76281980&t=130)** Each member of a group is waiting for some other member to take action, and as a result, neither member is able to make progress.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock?u=76281980&t=138)** Avoiding deadlock is a common challenge in concurrent programs that use mutual exclusion mechanisms to protect critical sections of code.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock?u=76281980&t=146)** We want our program to be free from deadlock to guarantee liveness, which is a set of properties that require concurrent programs to make progress.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock?u=76281980&t=155)** Some processes or threads may have to take turns in a critical section, but a well-written program with liveness guarantees that all processes will eventually make progress.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock?u=76281980&t=166)** But we're clearly not making any progress here.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock?u=76281980&t=170)** And I'm getting hungry.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock?u=76281980&t=171)** - Eureka, I have another idea. - Oh, do tell, do tell.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock?u=76281980&t=175)** - Well, our deadlock occurred because we both reached for the chopstick closest to us, first.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock?u=76281980&t=181)** I grabbed this chopstick first and you grabbed this chopstick first.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock?u=76281980&t=184)** That set us up for deadlock, but if we prioritize these locks so that we both try to acquire this chopstick first, then we won't have this problem because we'll both be competing for the same first lock.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock?u=76281980&t=200)** - Well, let's give that a try.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock?u=76281980&t=203)** Ah, brilliant.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock?u=76281980&t=205)** If you acquire the first chopstick and then I try to acquire it, I'll just be stuck here waiting until you finish taking your sushi and release both locks.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock?u=76281980&t=214)** Then I can grab that chopstick and this one, and take my turn, in the critical section.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock?u=76281980&t=221)** - In this example, we had two separate locks protecting a single-shared resource.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock?u=76281980&t=226)** That may not be the most realistic scenario, but it demonstrates the point.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock?u=76281980&t=231)** Now, imagine something like a banking application with a set of bank accounts where each one has its own mutex, to ensure that only one thread will be withdrawing from or depositing funds to that account at a time.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock?u=76281980&t=245)** To transfer funds between two accounts, a thread needs to acquire the locks for both the sender and the receiver, since it would be modifying the value of both accounts.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock?u=76281980&t=256)** If there are multiple threads concurrently making transfers between the accounts, then there's a real chance that they could end up competing for the same locks and run into this sort of deadlock scenario.

#### Deadlock: C++ demo
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] To demonstrate a deadlock, we've created this example program that simulates two dining philosophers thinking and eating sushi.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=9)** The philosopher function on line nine takes two reference parameters named first_chopstick and second_chopstick, which indicate the order in which the philosopher will lock the two mutexes.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=21)** The while loop on line 10 will continue to run as long as the sushi_count variable is positive, which represents the amount of sushi left on the shared plate between the philosophers.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=32)** We initialized the sushi count to 5000 on line seven, so our two philosophers should both end up very well fed.

> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=40)** Within the while loop, the philosopher will pick up and acquire a lock on their first_chopstick, followed by their second_chopstick, at which point, they're in the critical section.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=50)** If there's still sushi left on the plate, they'll take a piece by decrementing the sushi_count variable line 14, then finally unlock both chopsticks to put them down for someone else to take.

> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=61)** Down in the main function, we initialize two mutexes named chopstick_a and chopstick_b, then start two philosopher threads named barron and olivia.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=72)** Notice that each philosopher has a different first and second chopstick.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=76)** Barron will acquire chopstick_a first and then b, whereas Olivia acquires chopstick_b first and then a.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=85)** After both philosophers have eaten all of the sushi, their threads will finish and the program will print out a final message on line 27.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=94)** Switching over to the command prompt, when I run this program, it should take less than a second for our philosophers to eat 5000 pieces of sushi, but I don't get that final output message.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=106)** Our philosophers are stuck in a deadlock.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=109)** If I press control, shift, escape to open the Task Manager and then go to the Performance tab, I see that the CPU is barely being used.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=119)** Since the threads are stuck waiting on each other, the deadlocked program doesn't use any CPU cycles.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=126)** Now, my program will be stuck in this state forever, so I'll need to manually terminate it by pressing control break.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=134)** I could try running the program again and hope the two philosopher threads get scheduled differently this time, are lucky enough to avoid deadlocking, and make it to the end of the sushi plate.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=145)** But luck is not a good strategy for programming, so let's implement Olivia's solution of prioritizing the locks.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=152)** We'll say that Chopstick A has the highest priority, and B is second.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=158)** Each philosopher should always take their highest priority chopstick first.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=164)** I see that on line 24, Olivia takes chopstick_b as her first_chopstick and chopstick_a as her second one, so I'll swap those around.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=180)** Now both threads will acquire the two chopsticks in the same order.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=186)** If I make and run the program now, it reaches the end and prints the final message that our sushi-filled philosophers are done eating.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=199)** Now, we designed this example to be as simple as possible by only including a single shared resource, the sushi plate.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=206)** In practice, this program only really needs one lock to protect it.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=211)** We intentionally created the potential for deadlock because we nested two locks inside of each other to demonstrate the concept.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=218)** As your programs grow in complexity to include more critical sections, locks, and parallel threads with intertwined dependencies, it becomes increasingly more difficult to identify and prevent potential deadlocks.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=231)** The simplest technique to prevent deadlocks is the one we used in this video, which is to ensure that locks are always taken in the same order by every thread.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=241)** However, lock ordering may not always be feasible.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=245)** For example, a thread may not know all of the locks it will need to acquire ahead of taking any of them.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=252)** For our dining philosophers example, another possible solution in C++ is to use a scoped_lock.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=259)** When you create a scoped_lock object, it takes ownership of one or more mutexes and holds onto them for the scope in which that object was created.
>
> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=268)** The scoped_lock has a built-in deadlock avoidance algorithm when it's used to acquire multiple mutexes at once.
>
> **[4:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=275)** Back in our code, a return line 24 to its original state, which creates the potential for a deadlock.
>
> **[4:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=289)** Then I'll replace lines 11 and 12, which lock the first and second chopsticks with the constructor for a new scoped_lock.
>
> **[5:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=312)** Creating this scoped lock at the beginning of the while loop will acquire both mutexes and hold onto them until the end of that while loop, at which point the scoped lock is destructed and the mutexes are released.
>
> **[5:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=325)** They'll be automatically unlocked so I don't need these two unlock statements.
>
> **[5:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/deadlock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=335)** Switching back to the console, I'll make and then run the program again, and it finishes right away, but this time, it's using the C++ scoped_lock mechanism to avoid a deadlock rather than ordering the locks by priority.

>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/abandoned-lock?u=76281980&t=10)** I'm ready for another piece of sushi, so I'll pick up the first chopstick, then the second one, and I think I left the refrigerator open.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/abandoned-lock?u=76281980&t=21)** - Well, that was rude of him.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/abandoned-lock?u=76281980&t=23)** We've entered another form of a deadlock through thread death.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/abandoned-lock?u=76281980&t=27)** If one thread or process acquires a lock and then terminates because of some unexpected reason, it may not automatically release the lock before it disappears.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/abandoned-lock?u=76281980&t=37)** That leaves other tasks like me stuck waiting for a lock that will never be released and getting hungry.

> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/abandoned-lock?u=76281980&t=47)** Let's look at some code.

>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/abandoned-lock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=10)** In this version, we still have two philosophers eating and thinking, but we've consolidated the two mutexes into a single mutex simply named chopsticks.

>

>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/abandoned-lock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=54)** To simulate that happening, I'll add another If statement that checks to see if there are exactly 10 pieces of sushi left.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/abandoned-lock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=67)** And if so, the thread will print a message that this philosopher has had enough.

> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/abandoned-lock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=83)** And then after that, they'll use a break statement to immediately break out of the while loop before unlocking the chopstick.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/abandoned-lock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=94)** When I make and run that program, I get the message that one of the philosophers has had enough, but the other philosopher never finishes eating the last few pieces of sushi.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/abandoned-lock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=109)** That other thread is stuck waiting on a mutex that will never get unlocked, so the program will be stuck here forever.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/abandoned-lock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=116)** This scenario is not the same as the deadlock we looked at previously in this course because the two threads are not waiting on each other to release a lock, but it's a related scenario and the impact is the same.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/abandoned-lock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=129)** The program isn't making any progress, so I'll press Control + Break to terminate the process.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/abandoned-lock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=137)** A good way to avoid this problem with C++ is to use a mutex wrapper class like the scoped_lock.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/abandoned-lock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=144)** I'll replace the lock function on line 11 with the code to create a new scoped_lock object named lock using the chopsticks mutex,
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/abandoned-lock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=164)** and then delete the unlock function on line 19.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/abandoned-lock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=172)** The scoped_lock will only hold onto its lock of the mutex for the scope in which the scoped_lock object was created, which is the while loop iteration.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/abandoned-lock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=181)** If something happens to make the loop exit prematurely, like the break statement on line 17, the scoped_lock will automatically release its lock on the mutex for another thread to take.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/abandoned-lock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=194)** If I make and run this program using the scoped_lock, it's able to run until the end, even after one of the philosopher threads has had more than enough sushi.

#### Starvation
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/starvation?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/starvation?u=76281980&t=1)** - It would be nice if Olivia and I took turns acquiring and releasing the pair of chopsticks so we could each take an equal amount of sushi from the shared plate.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/starvation?u=76281980&t=11)** But that's not guaranteed to happen.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/starvation?u=76281980&t=13)** The operating system decides when each of our threads get scheduled to execute, and depending on the timing of that, it can lead to problems.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/starvation?u=76281980&t=21)** If Olivia puts down the chopsticks to release her lock on the critical section, but my thread doesn't get a chance to acquire them before she takes them again, then I'll be stuck waiting again until she takes another piece.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/starvation?u=76281980&t=35)** If that happens occasionally, it's probably not a big deal, but if it happens regularly,
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/starvation?u=76281980&t=41)** - Too slow.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/starvation?u=76281980&t=42)** - then my thread's going to starve.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/starvation?u=76281980&t=44)** Starvation occurs when a thread is unable to gain access to a necessary resource and is therefore unable to make progress.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/starvation?u=76281980&t=52)** If another greedy thread is frequently holding a lock on the shared resource, then the starved thread won't get a chance to execute.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/starvation?u=76281980&t=62)** - In a simple scenario like ours, with two equal threads competing for execution time, starvation probably isn't a concern.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/starvation?u=76281980&t=70)** Both of our threads should get plenty of chances to take sushi.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/starvation?u=76281980&t=74)** However, if our two threads are given different priorities, then that may not be the case.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/starvation?u=76281980&t=80)** Baron knows I get grumpy when I don't eat.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/starvation?u=76281980&t=82)** - And I certainly don't want that, so I'll give Olivia's thread a higher priority.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/starvation?u=76281980&t=86)** - Thanks.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/starvation?u=76281980&t=87)** - How different thread priorities get treated will depend on the operating system, but generally higher priority threads will be scheduled to execute more often, and that can leave low priority threads like me feeling hungry.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/starvation?u=76281980&t=101)** Another thing that can lead to starvation is having too many concurrent threads.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/starvation?u=76281980&t=106)** - Oh, I forgot to mention I invited some friends over.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/starvation?u=76281980&t=109)** - Well with this many competing threads, I may never get any sushi.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Speakers:** - it (1), - too (1), - then (1), - in (1), - and (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (1)
> **Code Keywords:** case. (1)

#### Starvation: C++ demo

>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/starvation-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=101)** I'll make and then run the program with that change.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/starvation-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=106)** And as I scroll through the final counts for each thread, I can see that some philosophers got a decent amount of sushi, but a lot of them didn't get any at all.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/starvation-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=117)** This one philosopher here took over 1,000.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/starvation-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=120)** All of these threads were created with the same default priority, but because there were so many threads, some never got scheduled to execute.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/starvation-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=128)** This represents a real problem in programs containing a large number of threads.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/starvation-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=133)** For example, if instead of feeding sushi to a bunch of hungry philosophers, this program was a web server that created new threads to handle a huge number of incoming requests, some of those requests may never get processed, and that would lead to some impatient and angry users on the other end.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/starvation-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=151)** There are techniques that can be used to improve or even guarantee fairness among threads, but that type of workload management is very situational-dependent and beyond the scope of this course.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** function (1), switch (1), let (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (2)
> **Tools:** command prompt (1)
> **Analogies:** for example (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### Livelock
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/livelock?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/livelock?u=76281980&t=0)** - Our greed and competition for sushi has led us to a life of deadlocks and starvation.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/livelock?u=76281980&t=6)** There's only one piece of sushi left, and I can see you're still hungry, you should have it.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/livelock?u=76281980&t=12)** - Thank you, my dear, but I can see you're still hungry too, and I would feel like a lousy husband if I allowed my wife to go hungry.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/livelock?u=76281980&t=20)** You should have the last bite.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/livelock?u=76281980&t=23)** - Oh, but I can't bear to see you hungry.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/livelock?u=76281980&t=26)** You shall have the final bite.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/livelock?u=76281980&t=29)** - Well, this is annoying.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/livelock?u=76281980&t=31)** We've entered into another tricky situation known as a livelock.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/livelock?u=76281980&t=36)** A livelock looks similar to a deadlock in the sense that two threads are blocking each other from making progress, but the difference is that the threads in a livelock are actively trying to resolve the problem.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/livelock?u=76281980&t=49)** A livelock can occur when two or more threads are designed to respond to the actions of each other.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/livelock?u=76281980&t=54)** Both threads are busy doing something, but the combination of their efforts prevent them from actually making progress and accomplishing anything useful.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/livelock?u=76281980&t=62)** The program will never reach the end.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/livelock?u=76281980&t=65)** The ironic thing about livelocks is that they're often caused by algorithms that are intended to detect and recover from deadlock.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/livelock?u=76281980&t=72)** If one or more process or thread takes action to resolve the deadlock, then those threads can end up being overly polite and stuck in a livelock.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/livelock?u=76281980&t=81)** To avoid that, ensure that only one process takes action, chosen by priority or some other mechanism, like random selection.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/livelock?u=76281980&t=89)** - Rock, paper, scissors for it?
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/livelock?u=76281980&t=92)** - One, two, three, shoot!
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/livelock?u=76281980&t=93)** - I win!
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/livelock?u=76281980&t=94)** - Now Olivia gets the last piece of sushi, and that resolves our livelock.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Speakers:** - our (1), - thank (1), - oh (1), - well (1), - rock (1)
> **Definitions:** known as (1)
> **Analogies:** similar to (1)

>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/livelock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=163)** This time, our threads that are trying to avoid a deadlock are instead caught in a livelock.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/livelock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=169)** With four philosophers grabbing for the chopsticks, by the time a philosopher has launched their first chopstick and goes for the second one, another philosopher already has it.

> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/livelock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=187)** So I'll need to manually terminate this program by pressing control break.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/livelock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=193)** To resolve the livelock, I'll use the thread's yield function, which will reschedule its execution to allow other threads to run.

> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/livelock-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=201)** Exactly how yield accomplishes that will vary depending on its implementation and the operating system scheduler.

### 6. Synchronization

#### Condition variable
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable?u=76281980&t=0)** - Baron and I just made a slow cooker full of delicious hot soup and I'm ready to dig in.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable?u=76281980&t=6)** - But we should take turns to make sure we each get our fair share of soup.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable?u=76281980&t=11)** In this scenario, we're two hungry threads competing for access to a shared resource: the soup.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable?u=76281980&t=17)** And the slow cooker lid will act as a mutex to protect it.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable?u=76281980&t=21)** Only the thread that holds the lid can check to see how much soup is left, determine if it's their turn to take the next serving, and modify the amount of soup that's left by taking some.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable?u=76281980&t=32)** - Mm, that was some really good soup.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable?u=76281980&t=34)** I think I'll have another serving.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable?u=76281980&t=38)** Oh, I see you haven't taken your share yet.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable?u=76281980&t=41)** What about now?
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable?u=76281980&t=43)** How about now?
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable?u=76281980&t=44)** Now?
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable?u=76281980&t=45)** - Olivia's thread is wasting a lot of energy repeatedly acquiring the mutex to check for a certain condition to see if it's her turn to take more soup, and she'll continue doing that until my thread eventually gets scheduled, so I can acquire the lid, see that it's my turn, and take my serving.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable?u=76281980&t=64)** - What I was doing is called busy waiting or spinning, repeatedly acquiring and releasing the lock to check for a certain condition to continue.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable?u=76281980&t=73)** It isn't very efficient, especially if it goes on for a long time.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable?u=76281980&t=78)** - This is one of the limitations of using just a mutex.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable?u=76281980&t=81)** Sure, it restricts multiple threads from taking soup at the same time, but the mutex alone doesn't give our threads a way to signal each other to synchronize our actions.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable?u=76281980&t=90)** To do that, we can use another mechanism called a condition variable, which serves as a queue or container for threads that are waiting for a certain condition to occur.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable?u=76281980&t=101)** Think of it as a place for threads to wait and be notified.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable?u=76281980&t=105)** The condition variable is associated with a mutex, and they work together to implement a higher level construct called a monitor.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable?u=76281980&t=113)** Monitors protect a critical section of code with mutual exclusion, and they provide the ability for threads to wait or block until a certain condition has become true, along with a mechanism to signal those waiting threads when their condition has been met.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable?u=76281980&t=129)** Conceptually, you can think of a monitor like a room that contains the procedures and shared data that you want to protect.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable?u=76281980&t=136)** Only one thread can be in that room at a time to use those procedures and access the data.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable?u=76281980&t=142)** The mutex is a lock on the door.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable?u=76281980&t=145)** Other threads that try to enter the protected section while it's occupied will wait outside in a condition variable, which is like a waiting room.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable?u=76281980&t=153)** They might all be waiting for the same condition to occur before they enter the monitor room, or there might be multiple condition variables or multiple waiting rooms waiting for different conditions to occur to acquire that same mutex.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable?u=76281980&t=168)** When the thread inside the monitor finishes its business in the critical section, it will signal one of the conditions that it's their turn to execute.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable?u=76281980&t=176)** Then, it releases its lock on the door to exit the critical section.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable?u=76281980&t=180)** One of the threads waiting for that condition that was signaled will wake up and take its turn in the monitor, locking the door behind it so it can execute the critical section.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable?u=76281980&t=190)** Now, the condition variable has three main operations: wait, signal, and broadcast.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable?u=76281980&t=197)** Before using a condition variable, I first need to acquire the mutex associated with it, check for my condition, I see that it's not my turn to take more soup, so I'll use the condition variables wait operation, which releases my lock on the mutex, and then puts my thread to sleep or a pause state and places it into a queue waiting for another thread to signal that somebody else takes soup.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable?u=76281980&t=224)** - Since Baron released his lock on the lid before going to sleep, now I can acquire it.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable?u=76281980&t=230)** See that it's my turn to take some soup, so I'll do that.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable?u=76281980&t=237)** Then I'll use the signal operation to wake up a single thread from the waiting queue so it can acquire the lock.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable?u=76281980&t=244)** Depending on the language you're using, you'll also see this operation called notify or wake.
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable?u=76281980&t=251)** Baron, wake up, it's your turn to take some soup.
>
> **[4:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable?u=76281980&t=255)** Finally, I'll release my lock on the mutex.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable?u=76281980&t=259)** - Ah, my turn.
>
> **[4:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable?u=76281980&t=261)** The third condition variable operation broadcast is similar to the signal operation except that it wakes up all of the threads in the waiting queue.
>
> **[4:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable?u=76281980&t=271)** You may also see it called things like notify all or wake all.
>
> **[4:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable?u=76281980&t=275)** Now, in this little scenario, we only had two threads signaling each other on a single condition, that somebody took soup, which then changes whose turn it is to take the next serving.
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable?u=76281980&t=286)** A more common use case that requires multiple condition variables is implementing a shared queue or buffer.
>
> **[4:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable?u=76281980&t=294)** If multiple threads will be putting items in a queue and taking them out, then it needs a mutex to ensure that only one thread can add or remove items from it at a time.
>
> **[5:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable?u=76281980&t=303)** And for that we can use two condition variables.
>
> **[5:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable?u=76281980&t=307)** If a thread tries to add an item to the queue, which is already full, then it can wait on a condition variable to indicate when the buffer is not full.
>
> **[5:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable?u=76281980&t=316)** And if a thread tries to take an item, but the queue's empty, then it can wait on another condition variable for buffer not empty.
>
> **[5:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable?u=76281980&t=325)** These condition variables enable threads to signal each other when the state of the queue changes.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Speakers:** - baron (1), - but (1), - mm (1), - olivia (1), - what (1)
> **Code Keywords:** continue (2), protected (1), finally, (1)
> **Definitions:** is called (1), is a  (1)
> **Analogies:** think of it as (1), similar to (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (1)

#### Condition variable: C++ demo
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] This C++ program to demonstrate using a condition variable has a function named hungry_person on line 10, which has an input parameter for an ID number.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=11)** The hungry_person function will run as a thread that alternates with other hungry_people threads to take servings of soup until it's all gone.

>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=84)** At the end of the hungry_person function, we print a message with that value on line 20 to see what happened.

>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=105)** The two threads will alternate taking servings of soup and then at the end, we can see that Person 0 put the lid back over a thousand times.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=114)** That's a lot of wasted cycles checking to see that it's not their turn for soup, so let's use a condition variable to help them coordinate.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=124)** The typical usage pattern for a condition variable involves first locking the mutex that we'll be using with the condition variable, and then using a while loop to check for some condition.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=136)** If the condition is not true, then we need to wait.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=140)** Calling the condition variable's wait function will release this thread's lock on the mutex and cause it to wait here.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=147)** Now I want to emphasize here that the condition variable is not the condition itself or an event.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=155)** The condition that we're checking for is the logic of the while loop.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=159)** Is it this thread's turn to take soup?
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=162)** The condition variable is just a place or mechanism for threads to wait.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=167)** When the waiting thread gets signaled by another thread, it will wake up, lock the mutex, and then check the while loop's condition again.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=175)** If the condition is true this time, then we'll continue past the loop to execute the critical section of code.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=183)** Now, one important reason for placing the condition variable's wait function inside of a wild loop like this is that in certain operating environments, the condition variable could have what's called a spurious wake up, meaning it wakes up from its waiting state when it's not supposed to.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=200)** By placing it inside of a while loop, if a spurious wake up occurs, the thread will see that it's still not time to continue on and it will go back to sleeping.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=210)** To implement that in our hungry_person function, we'll include the condition variable header at the top of our program.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=220)** Then we'll create a new condition variable object on line 10 named soup_taken.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=230)** Next, we'll change the if statement on line 16 into a while loop and we'll modify the condition it checks for to see if it's not this hungry_person's turn to take soup.
>
> **[4:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=242)** If it's not their turn, then we'll have the thread wait on the condition variable to get signaled after another thread takes soup.
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=258)** Notice that we're passing in the lid_lock so that the weight function knows which lock to release and then reacquire later when this thread gets signaled.
>
> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=268)** We'll also move incrementing the put_lid_back counter into the while loop to track how many times each thread has to wait because it was not their turn.
>
> **[4:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=279)** I'll close out the while loop and then clean up this hanging else statement.
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=287)** Now if the thread checks the condition and it is their turn, then execution will continue past the while loop.
>
> **[4:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=295)** We need to add another check to make sure that there is still soup left.
>
> **[5:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=304)** If there is soup left, the thread will take soup by decrementing these soup_servings and then finally it will need to signal the other thread to wake up.
>
> **[5:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=314)** To do that, we'll release our lock on the lid and then call the condition variable's notify_one function to signal the other hungry person that soup was taken, so it should be their turn.
>
> **[5:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=334)** After saving those changes, I'll switch back over to the console to build and then run the program, and now we have two hungry threads that are taking turns and coordinating their actions to avoid wasting a whole lot of energy unnecessarily checking whose turn it is.
>
> **[5:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=353)** Now let's see what happens if we expand this dinner party to include more hungry_people by modifying the program's main function to create five hungry_people threads instead of just two.
>
> **[6:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=368)** We'll also need to modify the condition statement on line 16 to rotate servings among the five people.
>
> **[6:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=378)** Now if I build and try to run the program again, it gets stuck, so I'll press control break to manually terminate the program.
>
> **[6:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=391)** The problem here is that we used the notify_one function on line 23, which only wakes up one of the waiting threads.
>
> **[6:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=399)** Of those four other threads, if it doesn't wake up the one whose turn it is next, then the program will get stuck.
>
> **[6:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=406)** The fix here is to change that function to notify_all, to wake up all of the waiting threads to check and see if it's their turn.
>
> **[7:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=420)** Now when I build and run the program, everything works great.
>
> **[7:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=425)** All of the threads eat and they take turns doing so.
>
> **[7:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/condition-variable-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=429)** If you only need to signal one waiting thread and you don't care which one it is, then the basic notify_one function will work fine, but in this example, since we wanted a specific thread to wake up and see that it's their turn, relying on the single notify_one function to wake up the right thread will not always work.

#### Producer-consumer

#### Producer-consumer: C++ demo
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/producer-consumer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980)

> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/producer-consumer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=34)** We're simply using integers here to represent the bowls of soup.

>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/producer-consumer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=98)** If so, it prints a message saying how much soup it ate.

>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/producer-consumer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=109)** If the bowl the consumer took was not the negative one indicator, then it adds the bowl's value to the soup_eaten counter on line 42 before looping back around to take another one.

>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/producer-consumer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=133)** Switching over to a console.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/producer-consumer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=135)** I've already built the program, so I'll run it and it errors out leaving me with a stack dump.

> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/producer-consumer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=143)** We're running into problems here because the C++ standard library queue is not thread safe, meaning it does not have any built-in mechanisms to protect it from data races and other problems when multiple threads are pushing and popping items on and off of it.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/producer-consumer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=159)** So let's create our own thread safe queue class by modifying the ServingLine.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/producer-consumer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=165)** To do that, I'll include the mutex and condition_variable headers up top.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/producer-consumer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=177)** Then I'll instantiate two more private members in the ServingLine class, a mutex, which I'll name after the ladle olivia and I use to serve soup.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/producer-consumer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=189)** And then a condition_variable, which we'll use to signal when a new bowl of soup is served.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/producer-consumer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=202)** Now up in the serve_soup function before pushing a bowl onto the soup_queue at line 12, let's protect it by instantiating a unique lock object using the ladle mutex.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/producer-consumer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=227)** Then after pushing soup onto the queue, we're done with the critical section, so we'll unlock the ladle to make it available for another thread to take.
>
> **[4:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/producer-consumer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=242)** Then we'll notify a consumer thread waiting on the soup_served condition_variable to let them know that soup was served.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/producer-consumer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=252)** That completes the serve_soup function used by the producer threads.
>
> **[4:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/producer-consumer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=255)** So let's fix up the take_soup function on line 18 for the consumer threads.
>
> **[4:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/producer-consumer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=261)** We'll start by creating a unique lock on the ladle mutex, similar to what we did in the serve_soup function.
>
> **[4:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/producer-consumer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=267)** So I'll simply copy and paste that line.
>
> **[4:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/producer-consumer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=273)** Then we'll use a while loop to wait while the soup_queue is empty because there's nothing to take.
>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/producer-consumer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=285)** If the queue is empty, we'll use the condition_variables weight function to release the ladle lock and wait here until another producer thread adds soup to the queue and then uses the notify_one function on line 15 to let us know that there's soup ready to take.
>
> **[5:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/producer-consumer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=303)** I'd like to point out here that we're using a unique lock in the serve_soup and take_soup functions instead of other types of C++ locks like lock_guard or scoped_lock because the condition_variables weight function only accepts a unique_lock, which can be unlocked and then relock later to transfer ownership between threads.
>
> **[5:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/producer-consumer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=324)** Switching back to the console, I'll build the program.
>
> **[5:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/producer-consumer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=329)** And then run it.
>
> **[5:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/producer-consumer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=333)** And it works.
>
> **[5:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/producer-consumer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=334)** We get messages from the two consumer threads that ate different amounts of soup, and together they consumed the total 10,000 bowls of soup that were served up by the producer thread.
>
> **[5:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/producer-consumer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=345)** Now you might be wondering why the C++ standard queue isn't thread safe by default after all other languages like Java and Python include thread safe queues as part of their standard libraries.
>
> **[5:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/producer-consumer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=358)** C++ gives you the basic building blocks to implement exactly what you need.
>
> **[6:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/producer-consumer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=363)** If you were using a queue for a single threaded application, then you would not want the additional overhead that comes with including a mutex and condition variable.
>
> **[6:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/producer-consumer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=372)** But we needed those mechanisms in place for our scenario, and fortunately it wasn't too difficult to create our own thread safe ServingLine class.
>
> **[6:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/producer-consumer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=382)** If you're not in the mood to write your own version of a queue class, then we recommend checking out the Boost C++ libraries, which include this thread safe queue to use with multiple readers and writers.

#### Semaphore
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] A semaphore is another synchronization mechanism that can be used to control access to shared resources, sort of like a mutex, but unlike a mutex, a semaphore can allow multiple threads to access the resource at the same time, and it includes a counter to track how many times it's been acquired or released.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore?u=76281980&t=21)** As long as the semaphore's count value is positive, any thread can acquire the semaphore, which then decrements that counter value.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore?u=76281980&t=29)** If the counter reaches zero, then threads trying to acquire the semaphore will be blocked and placed in a queue to wait until it's available.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore?u=76281980&t=38)** When a thread is done using the resource, it releases the semaphore, which increments that counter value, and if there are any other threads waiting to acquire the semaphore, there'll be signaled to wake up and do so.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore?u=76281980&t=50)** - Hmm, looks like my phone's about to die.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore?u=76281980&t=53)** - Lucky us.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore?u=76281980&t=55)** There's a charger right here.
