> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore?u=76281980&t=58)** This charger has two ports, so it can be used by up to two devices at the same time.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore?u=76281980&t=64)** You can think of the number of open ports as a semaphore.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore?u=76281980&t=67)** Right now, this semaphore has a value of two because there are two free ports, and when I acquire one of these ports by plugging in my phone, it decrements the semaphore's value to one.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore?u=76281980&t=81)** - I'll acquire the other port, and that decreases the semaphore's value to zero, which means it's unavailable for anyone else to use.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore?u=76281980&t=93)** - Lucky me.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore?u=76281980&t=94)** There's a charger right here.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore?u=76281980&t=95)** - Oh, man, sorry, Steve.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore?u=76281980&t=97)** The semaphore is locked right now because there aren't any available ports.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore?u=76281980&t=101)** You'll have to wait until one of us is done charging and releases the semaphore.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore?u=76281980&t=105)** - No worries, I'll wait.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore?u=76281980&t=107)** - You know, I don't need to charge that bad.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore?u=76281980&t=110)** I'll release my port, which increments the semaphore's value, and I'll notify Steve that it's available.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore?u=76281980&t=118)** Hey, Steve, wake up.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore?u=76281980&t=119)** - Cool, now the semaphore is positive, I can acquire it and charge my phone.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore?u=76281980&t=124)** - This type of semaphore that we're using here is called a counting semaphore because it can have a value of 0, 1, 2, 3, and so on to represent the number of resources we have.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore?u=76281980&t=135)** In our scenario, we're counting available charger ports, but in software, a counting semaphore might be used to manage access among multiple threads to a limited pool of connections for something like a server or a database, Or a counting semaphore could be used to track how many items are in a queue.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore?u=76281980&t=154)** Now, it's also common to restrict the possible values of a semaphore to only being either zero or one.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore?u=76281980&t=161)** With zero representing a locked state and one representing unlocked.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore?u=76281980&t=166)** This is called a binary semaphore, and at first glance, it looks a lot like a mutex.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore?u=76281980&t=172)** In fact, it can be used just like a mutex with a thread acquiring and releasing the semaphore to lock and unlock it.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore?u=76281980&t=180)** However, there is a key difference.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore?u=76281980&t=183)** A mutex can only be unlocked by the same thread that originally locked it.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore?u=76281980&t=189)** A semaphore on the other hand, can be acquired and released by different threads.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore?u=76281980&t=194)** Any thread can increment the semaphore's value by releasing it or attempt to decrement the value by acquiring it.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore?u=76281980&t=201)** That may sound like a recipe for trouble, and it certainly can be if you're not careful, but the ability for different threads to increment and decrement a semaphore's value and for threads to wait and be signaled by the semaphore is what enables it to be used as a signaling mechanism to synchronize the action between threads.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore?u=76281980&t=219)** For example, a pair of semaphores can be used in a similar way to condition variables to synchronize producer and consumer threads, adding and removing elements from a shared finite queue or buffer.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore?u=76281980&t=232)** One semaphore tracks the number of items in the buffer, shown here as fillCount, and the other one tracks the number of free spaces, which I'll call emptyCount.
>
> **[4:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore?u=76281980&t=242)** To add an element to the buffer, the producer will first acquire the emptyCount, which decrements its value, then it pushes the item into the buffer, and finally it releases the fillCount semaphore to increment its value.
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore?u=76281980&t=258)** Now, on the other side of the buffer, when the consumer wants to take an item, it first acquires fillCount to decrement its value, then it removes an item from the buffer, and finally increments the emptyCount by releasing it.
>
> **[4:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore?u=76281980&t=273)** Notice that the producer and consumer each acquire a different semaphore as the first operation in their respective sequences.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore?u=76281980&t=281)** If the consumer tries to take an item when the buffer is empty, then when it tries to acquire that fillCount semaphore, it'll block and wait until a producer adds an item and releases fillCount, which will then signal the consumer to continue.
>
> **[4:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore?u=76281980&t=296)** Likewise, if the producer tries to add an item to the fill buffer, then it goes to acquire the emptyCount semaphore, it will block and wait until a consumer removes an item and releases the emptyCount.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Speakers:** - lucky (2), - [instructor] (1), - hmm (1), - nice (1), - i (1)
> **Code Identifiers:** fillcount (5), emptycount (5)
> **Definitions:** is called (2), is a  (1)
> **Analogies:** just like (1), for example (1)
> **Code Keywords:** continue (1)

>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=64)** This is similar to the use of a condition variable we saw in previous examples.

>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=79)** Below that, in the release function, after locking the mutex, the function increments the count variable on line 23, then releases the lock and notifies the condition variable.

>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=107)** Below that, the cell_phone function attempts to acquire the charger semaphore on line 39.

>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=123)** Once a cell_phone thread has acquired the semaphore, it prints a message that it's charging and then sleeps for a random amount of time from one to two seconds.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=133)** After that, the cell_phone prints a message that it's done charging and then releases the semaphore on line 44 to increment its value so another thread can acquire it down.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=145)** In the main section of the program, we use a simple for loop to create and start 10 cell_phone threads.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=151)** That's a lot of cellphones to charge at once.

> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=169)** At most, there will be four phones connected to the charger at any given time.

> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=175)** Now, instead of using it as a counting semaphore, if I change the initialization value for the semaphore on line 36 from four to just one, it'll act as a binary semaphore.

> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=189)** When I make and run this program, now only one thread at a time will be able to acquire the semaphore.

> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=201)** The way we're using the binary semaphore now with the same thread that acquires it also being the one to release, means it's basically acting the same as a mutex.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/semaphore-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=211)** In fact, we could replace the charger semaphore with a mutex in this particular program, and it would function in a similar way.

### 7. Barriers

#### Race condition
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/race-condition?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/race-condition?u=76281980&t=0)** - Data races and race conditions are two different potential problems in concurrent programs that people often confuse with each other, probably because they have similar-sounding names with the word race in them, data races can occur when two or more threads concurrently access the same memory location.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/race-condition?u=76281980&t=20)** If at least one of those threads is writing to or changing that memory value, that can cause the threads to override each other or read wrong values.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/race-condition?u=76281980&t=30)** - That's a pretty straightforward definition, which makes it possible to create automated tools to identify potential data races and code.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/race-condition?u=76281980&t=38)** And to prevent those data races, you need to ensure mutual exclusion for the shared resource.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/race-condition?u=76281980&t=44)** A race condition, on the other hand, is a flaw in the timing or ordering of a program's execution that causes incorrect behavior.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/race-condition?u=76281980&t=52)** In practice, many race conditions are caused by data races and many data races lead to race conditions, but those two problems are not dependent on each other.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/race-condition?u=76281980&t=62)** - It's possible to have data races without a race condition and race conditions without a data race.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/race-condition?u=76281980&t=68)** Olivia and I invited Steve and the gang over to play video games next weekend, so we need to figure out how many bags of chips we need to buy to keep them all fed.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/race-condition?u=76281980&t=78)** Our shopping list is the shared resource, and this pencil serves as a mutext to protect it.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/race-condition?u=76281980&t=83)** Only the person or thread with the pencil can view or modify the shopping list.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/race-condition?u=76281980&t=89)** - I'll go first.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/race-condition?u=76281980&t=91)** I see that our shopping list already has one bag of chips.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/race-condition?u=76281980&t=95)** With Steve and the gang coming over, I think we need three more.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/race-condition?u=76281980&t=99)** So one plus three, that means we need four bags.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/race-condition?u=76281980&t=109)** - Well, I always overestimate the amount of chips we need for a party, so I'm going to double that.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/race-condition?u=76281980&t=116)** I see we have four, two times four is eight.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/race-condition?u=76281980&t=125)** Great. We need eight.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/race-condition?u=76281980&t=127)** Now, let's rewind that and see how else those operations could have played out if our two threads got scheduled differently.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/race-condition?u=76281980&t=135)** (quirky music)
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/race-condition?u=76281980&t=139)** - I'll go first.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/race-condition?u=76281980&t=140)** - Hold on. I'll go first this time.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/race-condition?u=76281980&t=144)** I see one bag of chips, but I like to overestimate, so I'll double that.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/race-condition?u=76281980&t=149)** One times two is two.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/race-condition?u=76281980&t=158)** - Thanks. Now, I'll add three bags to that.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/race-condition?u=76281980&t=162)** Two plus three is five.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/race-condition?u=76281980&t=164)** Mm, five bags is less than the eight we calculated last time.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/race-condition?u=76281980&t=168)** - Oh, don't tell me we're not going to have enough chips for the party.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/race-condition?u=76281980&t=172)** - That's okay. We'll fix this. - Phew!
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/race-condition?u=76281980&t=175)** - Even though we're using this pencil as a mutext to protect against a data race, the potential for a race condition still exists because the order in which our threads execute is not deterministic.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/race-condition?u=76281980&t=188)** When deciding how many bags to buy, if my thread runs first to add three bags before Baron doubles it, that gives us eight.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/race-condition?u=76281980&t=196)** But if Baron runs first to double the original value before I add three bags, then we end up with five.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/race-condition?u=76281980&t=204)** - The race condition we created here is fairly straightforward.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/race-condition?u=76281980&t=208)** But in practice, race conditions can be really hard to discover, and that's because a program might run correctly for millions of times while you're building and testing it, so you think everything's fine.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/race-condition?u=76281980&t=219)** You release the finished program, and then one time, things happen to execute in a different order, and that causes an incorrect result.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/race-condition?u=76281980&t=227)** Unfortunately, there's not a single catch-all way to detect race conditions.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/race-condition?u=76281980&t=232)** Sometimes putting sleep statements at different places throughout your code can help to uncover potential race conditions by changing the timing and therefore order in which threads get executed.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/race-condition?u=76281980&t=243)** That said, race conditions are often a type of Heisenbug, which is a software bug that seems to disappear or alter its behavior when you try to study it.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/race-condition?u=76281980&t=253)** Running debuggers and doing things to affect the timing of your code in search of a race condition may actually prevent the race condition from occurring.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Speakers:** - that (2), - i (2), - data (1), - it (1), - well (1)
> **Code Keywords:** override (1), let (1), this. (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (2)
> **Non-Speech:** (quirky music) (1)

#### Race condition: C++ demo

>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/race-condition-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=15)** The barron_shopper function on line 17 calls the cpu_work function to do a little bit of CPU-intensive work before locking the shared mutex named pencil.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/race-condition-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=27)** Then, doubling the bags of chips on line 20, and printing a message.

>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/race-condition-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=56)** Notice that both the barron and olivia_shoppers lock the pencil before modifying the shared bag_of_chips variable.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/race-condition-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=64)** Since only one thread can read or write that variable at a time, this program is protected against having a data race, but is still vulnerable to a race condition.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/race-condition-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=75)** To show that, I'll run the program.

> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/race-condition-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=91)** The relative order in which the Olivia and Barron threads were scheduled to add and multiply the bags of chips was different this time, which gave us a different result.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/race-condition-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=101)** Run it again, and yet another answer.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/race-condition-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=105)** Again, the problem here is not a data race because we've guaranteed mutual exclusion by having the shoppers lock the pencil before modifying the bags of chips.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/race-condition-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=115)** However, there is a race condition here because the order in which these threads are scheduled to execute changes each time we run the code, which changes the final result.

#### Barrier
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier?u=76281980&t=0)** - To prevent our race condition from occurring, we need a way to synchronize our actions so we execute our respective multiplication and addition operations in the correct order, and we can do that with something called a barrier.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier?u=76281980&t=13)** A barrier is a stopping point for a group of threads that prevents them from proceeding into all or enough threads have reached the barrier.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier?u=76281980&t=21)** I like to think of threads waiting on a barrier, like players on a sports team coming together for a huddle.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier?u=76281980&t=28)** Before they join the huddle, the players might be doing other things, putting on their equipment or getting a drink of water.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier?u=76281980&t=34)** As they finish those individual activities, they join their teammates at the huddle.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier?u=76281980&t=39)** Players in the huddle wait there until all of their fellow teammates arrive.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier?u=76281980&t=43)** Then they all yell break, and then they scatter about to continue playing their game.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier?u=76281980&t=48)** - We can use a similar strategy here to solve our race condition, huddling together to synchronize when we each execute our operations to add and multiply items on the shopping list.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier?u=76281980&t=59)** I should complete my operation of adding three bags of chips to the list before we huddle together.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier?u=76281980&t=65)** Then afterwards, Baron can double the amount.

> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier?u=76281980&t=68)** - Sounds good.

> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier?u=76281980&t=70)** - I'm scheduled to execute first this time, so I'll acquire the pencil.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier?u=76281980&t=74)** I'll add my three bags of chips to the list
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier?u=76281980&t=83)** and release my lock on the pencil and then meet you at the huddle, don't leave me hanging.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier?u=76281980&t=88)** - I don't have anything to do before the huddle, so break.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier?u=76281980&t=92)** Now we're past the barrier, so I'll double the number of chips.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier?u=76281980&t=103)** That gives us eight, which is the right amount.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier?u=76281980&t=105)** By using a barrier, the order in which our threads actually get scheduled to execute doesn't matter because the barrier synchronizes us.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier?u=76281980&t=113)** Olivia always adds three bags before the barrier, and I multiply by two after it.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier?u=76281980&t=119)** If we were to run that program again, and I happen to get scheduled first, well, I don't have anything to do before the barrier, so I'll wait for Olivia.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier?u=76281980&t=128)** - When I eventually get scheduled to execute, I'll complete my operations, then join the barrier.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier?u=76281980&t=134)** - [Both] Break.

> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier?u=76281980&t=136)** - Now I'm free to continue doing whatever else I need to do.

#### Barrier: C++ demo
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] At the time of this recording, in early 2020, barriers were an experimental part of the C++ standard, though it looks like they're expected to be included as part of the official C++20 standard.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=14)** But until that happens and it gets implemented in common compiler libraries, we can turn to the well-known boost collection of open source C++ libraries, which conveniently does have a barrier we can use.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=28)** To demonstrate using a barrier in C++, we'll build on the previous example that demonstrated a race condition by creating 10 shopper threads named Olivia and Baron that either added or multiplied the number of chips to buy.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=43)** Without a barrier in place, this program has a race condition that produces a different final result each time we run it.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=51)** So let's use a barrier to make sure all five of the Olivia shopper threads execute their add operation before the five Barron shoppers multiply the bags of chips.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=62)** First, we'll need to include the boost thread barrier header file at the top of the program.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=71)** And then we can create a new barrier named fist bump.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=79)** The constructor takes an argument for the number of threads to wait at the barrier before it releases.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=85)** Since this program instantiates 10 sharper threads, five Barrons and five Olivia's, and we want all of them to arrive at the barrier together before the program continues, we'll give it an input of 10.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=100)** Now that we've created the barrier, it's time to figure out where to use it.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=106)** We're starting all of my shopper threads together and I can't control when each one will get scheduled to execute, but all we really care about here is making sure that all of the Olivia threads execute their addition operation before the Barron threads execute their multiplication.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=124)** So, we'll use the barrier to separate those operations.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=128)** The Olivia threads will execute their addition operations before waiting at the barrier, whereas the Barron threads will go straight to waiting at the barrier.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=139)** Once all 10 threads have arrived at the barrier and are waiting on it, the barrier will release and then the Barron threads will execute their multiplication operations.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=149)** This ensures we have the right order of operations every time the program executes.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=155)** To implement the behavior of the Olivia shopper, we'll add the code to wait at the barrier after adding three bags of chips on line 29 by calling fist bump.wait.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=167)** Now the scoped lock we created on line 28 will still be in scope at this point, so if this thread goes into waiting while still holding a lock on the pencil, no other threads will be able to acquire the lock to continue onwards, and the program will get stuck.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=184)** So, let's use a pair of curly brackets to restrict the scope of the scoped lock to adjust the critical section.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=197)** Next, up in the Barron shopper function, we'll put the fist bump.wait function before creating the scoped log and doubling the chips.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=207)** And finally, since we're using the boost libraries barrier function, we'll need to modify the make file to link the necessary boosts libraries.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=217)** Switching over to the make file, we'll create a new macro for the libraries to link and include boost thread and boost system.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=230)** Then we'll add that to the list of arguments for building the program.
>
> **[4:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=242)** Now, when we make and run this program, all of the Olivia shoppers execute their addition operation first, and then all of the Barron shoppers do their multiplication afterwards, that gives us a final total of 512 bags of chips.
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/barrier-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=258)** And if I run the program again, we'll always get the same answer.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** function (3), let (2), continue (1), finally, (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (4)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### Latch: C++ demo
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/latch-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/latch-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In addition to barriers to control the order of execution, there's another related synchronization mechanism called a latch.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/latch-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=8)** This allows one or more threads to wait until a set of operations being performed in other threads to complete.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/latch-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=15)** The latch is initialized with a given count value, which threads can use in two ways.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/latch-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=21)** Threads can either wait at a latch until the count value reaches zero.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/latch-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=25)** Much like how threads can wait at a barrier.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/latch-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=28)** Or they can decrement the count value by calling the latch's countdown function.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/latch-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=34)** So, the key difference between a latch and a barrier is that the barrier releases when a certain number of threads waiting on it has been reached.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/latch-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=43)** Whereas the latch releases after the countdown function gets called enough times to reduce the count value to zero.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/latch-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=52)** For this demonstration, we'll modify the previous example code that used a barrier to now use a latch instead.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/latch-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=60)** First, we'll change the include statement on line 6 to include the latch header file from the boost library.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/latch-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=68)** Then on line 10, we'll replace the barrier named fist_bump with the latch.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/latch-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=76)** And we'll initialize the countdown value to 5.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/latch-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=79)** Because there are five Olivia threads that need to execute before the other five Barron threads.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/latch-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=87)** Down in the olivia_shopper function, we'll replace the wait function on line 34 with the latch's countdown function.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/latch-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=98)** After each of the Olivia threads finishes adding their three bags of chips, they'll call this function to decrement the count value.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/latch-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=106)** And the Barron threads that are waiting at their wait function on line 21 will be released to continue when the count reaches zero.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/latch-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=114)** If the latch has already been released, when a Barron thread reaches it, it doesn't need to wait for the other Barron threads to arrive to continue.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/latch-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=122)** Switching over to the console, I'll recompile these changes, and then run the program.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/latch-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=130)** And we get the same result as with the barrier.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/latch-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=132)** We're just using a different mechanism to do it.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/latch-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=136)** Now, to demonstrate something that can go wrong when using latches, if we were to increase the initial count on line 10 to 10 instead of 5, make and run the program, now it gets stuck waiting at the latch because there are not enough Olivia threads calling the countdown function for it to reach zero.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/latch-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=162)** So, always make sure your program will be able to execute the countdown function enough times to avoid getting stuck like this.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/latch-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=170)** One way that latches are commonly used is with an initial count value of 1 to serve as a simple gate.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/latch-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=178)** Any number of threads can wait at the gate until another controlling thread executes the countdown function just once, which releases the waiting threads to continue on.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/latch-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=189)** Initializing the count to some other value N can be used to make one thread wait until N other threads have completed some action and calling the countdown function, or for some action to be completed N times.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/latch-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=203)** Perhaps by a single thread with a loop.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/latch-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=206)** Keep in mind that the latch doesn't require the threads calling countdown to wait there until proceeding.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/latch-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=212)** They're free to continue.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/latch-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=214)** It only prevents threads that call the wait function from proceeding before the count reaches zero.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

> **Best Practices:** always make sure (1)

### 8. Asynchronous Tasks

#### Computational graph
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/computational-graph?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/computational-graph?u=76281980&t=0)** - The key to parallel programming is determining which steps within a program can be executed in parallel and then figuring out how to coordinate them.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/computational-graph?u=76281980&t=10)** And one tool that help model how steps in a program relate to each other is a computational graph.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/computational-graph?u=76281980&t=16)** Consider the steps to make a very simple salad.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/computational-graph?u=76281980&t=19)** I'll need to chop some lettuce, chop some tomatoes, mix those chopped ingredients together, and then finally add salad dressing.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/computational-graph?u=76281980&t=27)** Each of those steps represents a task, which is a unit of execution or a unit of work.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/computational-graph?u=76281980&t=33)** I can draw those tasks as nodes in a graph and use arrows to indicate progression from one task to the next.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/computational-graph?u=76281980&t=39)** A task cannot begin executing until all of the tasks with arrows feeding into it have completed.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/computational-graph?u=76281980&t=46)** When those four tasks are arranged sequentially like this, they represent a single path of execution which could be implemented as a single thread or process.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/computational-graph?u=76281980&t=55)** If I do those steps in that order, I'll make a somewhat boring salad, but that's not the only ordering that could work.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/computational-graph?u=76281980&t=64)** The tasks of chopping lettuce and chopping tomatoes can occur asynchronously, meaning the order in which they happen relative to each other doesn't really matter.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/computational-graph?u=76281980&t=74)** I could chop the lettuce first or the tomatoes first or ideally, I'll chop them both in parallel to save some time.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/computational-graph?u=76281980&t=82)** So I'll add another node to the front of my diagram labeled Spawn, which has two output arrows leading into the two asynchronous chopping tasks.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/computational-graph?u=76281980&t=91)** Both tasks can begin running at any time after the Spawn operation.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/computational-graph?u=76281980&t=96)** Now, there is a dependency between those two chopping tasks and the third task because I can't mix the chopped ingredients together until both of the chopping tasks are complete.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/computational-graph?u=76281980&t=108)** My program will need some form of synchronization, so I'll add another node to represent that operation.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/computational-graph?u=76281980&t=114)** This Sync node will not be able to execute until both of the chopping tasks that feed into it are complete.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/computational-graph?u=76281980&t=121)** I've used the terms spawn and sync here, but you'll also see the terms fork and join used in this context.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/computational-graph?u=76281980&t=129)** The fact that there are not any direct edges or connections between the Chop Lettuce and Chop Tomato tasks indicates the possibility for parallel execution.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/computational-graph?u=76281980&t=139)** So if this program was implemented using two threads, as shown here, with a second thread spawning or forking from the main thread, the two chopping tasks can run at the same time.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/computational-graph?u=76281980&t=151)** This type of diagram is called a directed acyclic graph, or DAG.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/computational-graph?u=76281980&t=156)** Directed, because each edge is directed from one node or vertex to another, and acyclic, meaning it doesn't have any loops that cycle back on itself.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/computational-graph?u=76281980&t=166)** There are several variations on ways to draw these types of computational graphs, but their general purpose is to provide an abstract representation of the program.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/computational-graph?u=76281980&t=175)** They help to visualize the relationship and dependencies between tasks.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/computational-graph?u=76281980&t=179)** They can also be used to get a sense of how parallel a program can be.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/computational-graph?u=76281980&t=184)** Every node represents a task or operation, and for each one, I'll indicate the amount of time it takes to execute.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/computational-graph?u=76281980&t=191)** For this example, I'm just using made-up numbers and units of seconds.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/computational-graph?u=76281980&t=195)** If I add together the execution times for all of the nodes, that gives me a metric called Work, which represents the time it would take to execute all of these tasks on a single processor.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/computational-graph?u=76281980&t=207)** For this example, that comes out to 60 seconds.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/computational-graph?u=76281980&t=210)** Next, I'll identify the longest possible path through this graph, following the directed edges from node to node, which is referred to as the critical path.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/computational-graph?u=76281980&t=220)** It represents the longest series of sequential operations through the program.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/computational-graph?u=76281980&t=224)** If I add together the times for all of those nodes along the critical path, I get another metric called Span, which indicates the shortest possible execution time if this program was parallelized as much as possible.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/computational-graph?u=76281980&t=238)** In this case, that's 45 seconds.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/computational-graph?u=76281980&t=240)** The ratio of work to span indicates the ideal parallelism of this program, how much faster can the parallel version of this program possibly run using multiple processors than this sequential version running on just one processor.
>
> **[4:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/computational-graph?u=76281980&t=255)** The parallelism ratio of 1.33 means that at very best, the parallel version of this program will be 33% faster than the sequential version.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/computational-graph?u=76281980&t=265)** We may not be able to reduce the total amount of work a program needs to do, so minimizing the critical path is important in designing parallel algorithms, because span determines the shortest possible execution time.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **CLI Commands:** node (7), make (2)
> **Definitions:** is a  (3), is called (1), means that (1)
> **Code Keywords:** this, (1), abstract (1), case, (1)
> **Env Vars:** dag (1)
> **Versions:** 1.33 (1)
> **Speakers:** - the (1)

#### Thread pool
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-pool?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-pool?u=76281980&t=0)** - After identifying the tasks in a program that can run asynchronously, one way to run those tasks in parallel is to create independent threads or processes for each of them.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-pool?u=76281980&t=10)** Preparing a basic salad requires us to chop lettuce and chop tomatoes, so Olivia and I will act as independent threads to execute those tasks in parallel on our two processors, the knives,
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-pool?u=76281980&t=23)** - A salad with just lettuce and tomatoes is so boring.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-pool?u=76281980&t=27)** We need something more.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-pool?u=76281980&t=28)** What about cucumbers?
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-pool?u=76281980&t=30)** - Sure.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-pool?u=76281980&t=30)** Chopping cucumbers is another task that can run asynchronously, so we'll spawn another thread to handle that.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-pool?u=76281980&t=36)** - Hey, Baron.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-pool?u=76281980&t=38)** We need onions too.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-pool?u=76281980&t=39)** What's up, Olivia?
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-pool?u=76281980&t=40)** - Another task, another thread.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-pool?u=76281980&t=42)** - And mushrooms.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-pool?u=76281980&t=43)** - Again, another thread.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-pool?u=76281980&t=46)** - And carrots and celery and peppers, and an eggplant.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-pool?u=76281980&t=51)** - Whoa, whoa, whoa.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-pool?u=76281980&t=53)** It's getting crowded in here.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-pool?u=76281980&t=54)** We've got a lot of threads in the kitchen, but only two processors to execute on.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-pool?u=76281980&t=59)** That means a lot of threads will be standing around waiting their turn.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-pool?u=76281980&t=63)** Although threads are considered to be lightweight, every time we spawn a new thread, it does require some amount of overhead in terms of processor time and memory, or in this case, kitchen space.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-pool?u=76281980&t=74)** In some scenarios, rather than creating a new thread for every single task, it can be more efficient to use a thread pool, which creates and maintains a small collection of worker threads.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-pool?u=76281980&t=87)** As the program submits tasks to the thread pool, the thread pool reuses those existing worker threads to execute the task.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-pool?u=76281980&t=94)** Submitting tasks to a thread pool is like adding them to a to-do list for the worker threads.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-pool?u=76281980&t=100)** Now Olivia and I are two workers in a pool, and we have a queue of tasks or vegetables waiting for us to chop.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-pool?u=76281980&t=107)** After one of us finishes executing our current task, we'll take another one from the queue.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-pool?u=76281980&t=112)** - I'm done chopping the tomatoes, so I'll move on to the cucumbers.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-pool?u=76281980&t=116)** - Reusing threads with a thread pool addresses the overhead evolved with creating new threads, and that becomes a real advantage when the time it takes to execute the task is less than the time required to create a new thread.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-pool?u=76281980&t=129)** It doesn't take long to chop one of the vegetables, but it does take a long time to call up our friends to take on each of these individual tasks.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-pool?u=76281980&t=137)** - Since our threads already exist, when a new task arrives, we eliminate the delay of thread creation, which can make our program more responsive.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Speakers:** - and (2), - after (1), - a (1), - sure (1), - hey (1)
> **Code Keywords:** require (1), case, (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (1)

#### Thread pool: C++ demo
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-pool-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980)

>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-pool-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] C++ doesn't include thread pools as part of the C++ standard template library.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-pool-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=6)** So once again, we'll be using the open source Boost C++ libraries for this example for its thread pool class.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-pool-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=15)** To demonstrate using a thread pool, we've created this program which defines a function named vegetable_chopper on line six that simply prints a message that includes the current thread's ID number and a vegetable ID number, which gets passed as an input to the function.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-pool-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=31)** Down in the main section, we use a for loop on line 12 to create and start 100 vegetable chopper threads, followed by a second for loop to wait for them all to finish and join.

> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-pool-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=57)** Now let's accomplish the same task of chopping 100 vegetables, but do so with only a handful of threads in a thread pool.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-pool-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=65)** First, we'll replace the include statement for the thread header file with the boost/asio.hpp header file.
>

>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-pool-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=86)** Next, down in the main function, instead of instantiating an array to hold 100 threads on line 11, we'll create a new thread pool.

> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-pool-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=99)** This thread pool constructor can take an optional argument for the number of threads in the pool, so let's make this a pool with four threads in it.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-pool-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=109)** Now, within the for loop, rather than creating new threads, we'll submit the vegetable chopper function to the thread pool using the post function.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-pool-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=121)** We'll wrap the vegetable chopper function in a Lambda expression as the token we're submitting for the pool to execute.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-pool-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=134)** And after that, instead of using a second for loop to join each of the vegetable chopper threads individually, we can simply call the join function on the thread pool, which will wait until all of the submitted tasks have finished executing.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-pool-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=148)** Finally, since we're using the Boost thread pool in this example program, we'll need to modify the make file to link to the Boost Library.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-pool-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=162)** Now, when I rebuild and then run that program, the output still prints 100 messages because the program still chops 100 vegetables, but now the thread IDs are different.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-pool-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=178)** If you look closely, you'll see that the same four IDs are repeated over and over.

> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/thread-pool-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=183)** We're reusing the same four threads in the pool to execute all 100 tasks.

#### Future
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/future?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/future?u=76281980&t=0)** - Launching asynchronous tasks is a great way to accomplish multiple things at once.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/future?u=76281980&t=5)** Olivia, can you go check how many vegetables are in the pantry?
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/future?u=76281980&t=8)** - Sure, I can do that.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/future?u=76281980&t=9)** - While Olivia is busy asynchronously counting veggies, my thread is for you to continue doing other work.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/future?u=76281980&t=16)** But now she's gone and I need a way to get that result back from her when she's done.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/future?u=76281980&t=21)** This is where a mechanism called a future can be used.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/future?u=76281980&t=25)** A future acts as a placeholder for a result that's initially unknown but will be available at some point in the future.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/future?u=76281980&t=32)** It provides a mechanism to access the result of an asynchronous operation.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/future?u=76281980&t=37)** I like to think of a future like an IOU note for the result.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/future?u=76281980&t=41)** Hey, Olivia.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/future?u=76281980&t=42)** - Heyo.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/future?u=76281980&t=43)** - Hey.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/future?u=76281980&t=44)** I need you to check how many vegetables are in the pantry and give me back an answer.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/future?u=76281980&t=48)** - Sure, I promise to do that, and here's an IOU note that I'll get you that answer.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/future?u=76281980&t=52)** - Thank you.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/future?u=76281980&t=54)** Now I've got a handle to see that future result, and I'll hold onto it as I continue doing other work in the kitchen.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/future?u=76281980&t=60)** Eventually, I may reach a point in my work that I need the result from Olivia, perhaps to make a decision or complete some type of computation.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/future?u=76281980&t=68)** The future is read only, and I see that the result isn't ready yet so all I can do is wait until Olivia finishes.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/future?u=76281980&t=78)** When she finally does, she'll write the result value to that future, which is called resolving or fulfilling it.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/future?u=76281980&t=84)** She's fulfilling her promise to get me an answer.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/future?u=76281980&t=87)** And I see we have zero veggies left in the pantry.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/future?u=76281980&t=92)** - We chopped them all up making salads.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/future?u=76281980&t=94)** - Well, at least now I have an answer, and now I need to make a trip to the store.
