#### Divide and conquer
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer?u=76281980&t=0)** - One class of algorithms that are well-suited for parallel execution across multiple processors are divide and conquer algorithms.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer?u=76281980&t=9)** They work by first dividing a large problem into a number of smaller sub-problems of roughly equal size.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer?u=76281980&t=16)** Next, the conquer phase recursively solves each of those sub-problems.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer?u=76281980&t=20)** And finally, the solution to the sub-problems are combined together to produce the overall solution for the original problem.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer?u=76281980&t=28)** The common structure for divide and conquer code usually consists of an if-else statement.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer?u=76281980&t=34)** If the algorithm has reached what's called a base case, meaning the problem has been subdivided into a small enough piece to solve directly, then simply solve it.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer?u=76281980&t=43)** Otherwise, following the else case, divide the current problem into two smaller pieces referred to as the left and right problems.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer?u=76281980&t=51)** Solve both of those problems recursively using the same divide and conquer strategy, then combine the left and right solutions.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer?u=76281980&t=59)** Consider the task of summing together a large number of elements.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer?u=76281980&t=63)** I have an array of shopping receipts here and I need to add them all together to figure out how much we spent buying all those vegetables.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer?u=76281980&t=71)** If I were doing that alone as a sequential process, I would simply iterate through the receipts in the array and accumulate their values.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer?u=76281980&t=79)** Five plus 13 is 18 plus seven is 25 plus three is 28.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer?u=76281980&t=88)** - It's going to take forever.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer?u=76281980&t=90)** Let's divide and conquer this task.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer?u=76281980&t=92)** You add this half of receipts, and I'll add this half.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer?u=76281980&t=96)** We've subdivided this task into two subtasks that each of our threads can execute.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer?u=76281980&t=101)** - Do you want to subdivide it into even smaller parts?
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer?u=76281980&t=103)** - Sure, we can continue to recursively divide the receipts into smaller and smaller subgroups until we eventually reach our base case.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer?u=76281980&t=113)** For some algorithms, the base case may require you to continue recursively subdividing until you reach individual elements.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer?u=76281980&t=121)** But for our purpose, we can define our base case to stop when we reach a threshold amount of receipts.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer?u=76281980&t=128)** At that point, we'll add each of those subgroups of receipts together, then reverse or unwind the recursion to combine the results from all of the sub-problems to get our final answer.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer?u=76281980&t=139)** - My half of the receipts add up to 41.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer?u=76281980&t=142)** - And mine add up to 62.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer?u=76281980&t=144)** So we spent a total of $103 on groceries.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer?u=76281980&t=148)** Divide and conquer algorithms lend themselves to being made parallel because each of the sub-problems are independent, so they can be executed in parallel on different processors.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer?u=76281980&t=159)** - Now just because a divide and conquer algorithm can be made parallel doesn't mean it's always advantageous to do so.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer?u=76281980&t=166)** Depending on the size of the problem set and the complexity of the operations involved, the cost and overhead involved in making the algorithm parallel may outweigh the potential benefits.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** case, (2), continue (2), finally, (1), let (1), case. (1)
> **Speakers:** - one (1), - it (1), - do (1), - sure (1), - my (1)

#### Divide and conquer: C++ demo
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] To demonstrate a parallel divide and conquer algorithm in C++, we'll implement a function that sums together all of the integer values over a range between two input values low and high.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=14)** We will start with this single threaded implementation of a recursive sum algorithm on line six, which takes two input parameters, low and high values, representing the range of numbers to sum over.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=26)** The if statement on line seven looks at the difference between the low and high value to determine if the problem has been sufficiently subdivided.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=35)** If so, we've reached the base case and it returns the sum of numbers in that range.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=41)** Otherwise, the L statement beginning on line 13 determines the middle index between low and high.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=48)** Then recursively calls the recursive sum function on the low to middle index, which is referred to as the left half, and from the middle to high index, the right half.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=59)** Then it returns the sum of those left and right halves.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=64)** Down in the main function, this program simply calls recursive sum on line 22 for the range of zero to 1 billion, and then prints the result.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=74)** When I run the program, it gives me this really big number as the resulting total.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=82)** Now, let's modify this algorithm to take advantage of parallel execution by using futures.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=88)** To do that, we'll first need to include the future header file at the top of the program.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=96)** Next, we'll modify line 16 to calculate the recursive sum for the left half of the range asynchronously by launching it as an asynchronous task.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=113)** That call to the async function will spawn a new thread to execute recursive sum and return a future object, which gets held in the variable named left.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=123)** We don't need to do the same thing for the right half and spawn another asynchronous thread because we can just use the current thread to handle processing it.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=132)** At the end of the function, when it's time to add together the totals for the left and right halves, we'll need to get the return value from the left future by calling the get function on it.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=146)** Now, let's try building and running that program and it finishes without displaying any output, but we should have seen a final print statement with the end total.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=160)** So that means something has gone wrong here.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=164)** This program is trying to sum the numbers from zero to a billion, but up in the recursive sum function, our base case threshold will only sequentially sum together up to 100 numbers at a time.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=177)** That means this program will be subdividing the problem a lot and therefore spawning a lot of threads with each call to the async function.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=187)** There's a limit to the number of threads a program can create and that the operating system can manage and trying to spawn more threads than that will certainly cause problems.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=197)** Also, we should consider what these threads will actually be able to do.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=201)** The computer we're using only has four cores, so if we spawn a thousand threads, we'll have nearly a thousand threads waiting around for their turn on the processor.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=212)** That's way too many threads to be useful here, so let's limit the depth of recursion to only spawn a handful of threads.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=221)** To do that, we'll give the recursive sum function on line seven, an optional argument for depth with a default value of zero.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=230)** Then when we call this function recursively on lines 16 and 17, increment the depth of value for those recursive calls.
>
> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=246)** Finally, let's modify the if statement on line eight to use the depth of recursion to decide whether or not to execute the base case.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=256)** For simplicity here, we'll set the depth threshold to three, but in practice you might want to consider other factors like the number of processors in your system to determine a more appropriate threshold.
>
> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/divide-and-conquer-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=268)** Switching back to the console one last time, I'll make and then run the program, and now after it finishes, we get the expected message with the same output value as before, except now we're using parallel threads to calculate it.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

### 9. Evaluating Parallel Performance

#### Speedup, latency, and throughput
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/speedup-latency-and-throughput?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/speedup-latency-and-throughput?u=76281980&t=0)** - There are several reasons for using multiple processors to execute a program in parallel.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/speedup-latency-and-throughput?u=76281980&t=6)** One reason might be to increase the size of the problem you can tackle in a certain amount of time.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/speedup-latency-and-throughput?u=76281980&t=12)** For example, we're going to a party and I promise to bring 10 cupcakes.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/speedup-latency-and-throughput?u=76281980&t=17)** Working by myself, I can decorate 10 cupcakes in one hour.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/speedup-latency-and-throughput?u=76281980&t=21)** They're very fancy cupcakes.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/speedup-latency-and-throughput?u=76281980&t=23)** But if Baron joins me as a second processor doing the same type of work in parallel, together we can decorate 20 cupcakes in one hour.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/speedup-latency-and-throughput?u=76281980&t=32)** This type of parallelization is called weak scaling.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/speedup-latency-and-throughput?u=76281980&t=36)** We're keeping the size of the problem for each processor constant, but we're bringing in more processors to accomplish more work in the same amount of time.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/speedup-latency-and-throughput?u=76281980&t=45)** - Another reason for parallelization and bringing in more processors is to accomplish a given task faster.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/speedup-latency-and-throughput?u=76281980&t=52)** If Olivia promised to bring 10 cupcakes to the party, then working alone, it would take her one hour to decorate all of them.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/speedup-latency-and-throughput?u=76281980&t=60)** But if we split the workload, so she'll do half and I'll do half, then working together in parallel, we can decorate those 10 cupcakes in only about 30 minutes.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/speedup-latency-and-throughput?u=76281980&t=71)** This is called strong scaling, and it involves breaking down and spreading a problem across multiple processors to execute the program faster.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/speedup-latency-and-throughput?u=76281980&t=80)** In those two examples, we're using parallel processors to do more work in a set amount of time or do a set amount of work in less time.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/speedup-latency-and-throughput?u=76281980&t=90)** In either case, we're increasing the program's overall throughput.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/speedup-latency-and-throughput?u=76281980&t=94)** That is the number of tasks it can complete in a given amount of time.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/speedup-latency-and-throughput?u=76281980&t=99)** Throughput is related to another important metric called latency, which is the amount of time it takes to execute a task from beginning to end.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/speedup-latency-and-throughput?u=76281980&t=108)** Latency is measured in units of time, so if it takes six minutes to decorate one cupcake, that's a latency of six minutes.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/speedup-latency-and-throughput?u=76281980&t=117)** Throughput is expressed in actions per unit of time, so the throughput of one processor, that is Olivia working alone, is 10 cupcakes per hour.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/speedup-latency-and-throughput?u=76281980&t=128)** Two processors working in parallel will have the same latency of six minutes to decorate each cupcake, but their combined throughput increases to 20 cupcakes per hour, and with three processors, the throughput goes even higher to 30 cupcakes per hour.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/speedup-latency-and-throughput?u=76281980&t=143)** - A metric that's commonly used to measure the effectiveness of a parallel program is speed up, which is related to the program's efficiency.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/speedup-latency-and-throughput?u=76281980&t=152)** Speed up is calculated as a ratio of the time it takes to execute the program in the optimal sequential manner with just one worker, or a single processor, over the time it takes to execute in a parallel manner with a certain number of parallel processors.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/speedup-latency-and-throughput?u=76281980&t=168)** So if one worker takes an hour, or 60 minutes, to make 10 cupcakes, but two workers can do the same job in only 30 minutes, that corresponds to a speedup of two.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/speedup-latency-and-throughput?u=76281980&t=180)** If adding a third worker drops a time to 20 minutes, that's a speedup of three.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/speedup-latency-and-throughput?u=76281980&t=186)** - Now, our simplified cupcake example really represents a best case scenario, because a task like decorating cupcakes can be completely paralyzed among multiple workers, but in practice, that's rarely the case.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/speedup-latency-and-throughput?u=76281980&t=200)** It's more common to have programs where some parts can be parallelized, but other parts can't.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/speedup-latency-and-throughput?u=76281980&t=206)** Let's say at the end of our cupcake decorating program, we need to pack the finished cupcakes into this container.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/speedup-latency-and-throughput?u=76281980&t=212)** If only one of our threads can interact with the shared container at a time, we'll have to take turns using it.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/speedup-latency-and-throughput?u=76281980&t=218)** So that part of our program will have to execute sequentially, and that creates a limit on the amount of speed up we can possibly achieve.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Speakers:** - there (1), - another (1), - a (1), - now (1)
> **Code Keywords:** case, (1), case. (1), let (1)
> **Definitions:** is called (2)
> **CLI Commands:** make (1)
> **Analogies:** for example (1)

#### Amdahl's law
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/amdahl-s-law?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/amdahl-s-law?u=76281980&t=0)** - There's a well-known equation for estimating the speed up that a parallel program can achieve called Amdahl's Law, which is named after the computer scientist that formulated it.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/amdahl-s-law?u=76281980&t=10)** It's a way to estimate how much bang for your buck you'll actually get by parallelizing a program.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/amdahl-s-law?u=76281980&t=16)** In this equation for Amdahl's Law, P represents the portion of a program that can be made parallel, and S is the speed up for that parallelized portion of the program running on multiple processors.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/amdahl-s-law?u=76281980&t=29)** So for this example, if 95% of our cupcake decorating program can be executed in parallel and doing so with two processors produces a speed up of two for that part of the program, then the theoretical speed up for the entire program is about 1.9, which is a little bit less than two.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/amdahl-s-law?u=76281980&t=49)** If we add a third processor to increase the speed up for the parallelized portion to three, then the overall speed up will be around 2.7.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/amdahl-s-law?u=76281980&t=58)** Using 4 processors gives us a speed up of 3.5, and so on.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/amdahl-s-law?u=76281980&t=63)** Now, let's say we spend a lot of money and buy a computer with a thousand processing cores.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/amdahl-s-law?u=76281980&t=69)** Instinctively, I would expect that to give a speed up of somewhere at least close to a thousand, that'd be great, but according to Amdahl's Law, the overall speed up with a thousand processors will only be around 19.6.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/amdahl-s-law?u=76281980&t=83)** If I go wild and bump it up to a million processors, the overall speed up only increases to slightly less than 20.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/amdahl-s-law?u=76281980&t=91)** - 95% of our program is parallelizable, but the 5% that's not, the part of the program that has to execute sequentially, that's creating an upper limit on the speed up we can achieve.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/amdahl-s-law?u=76281980&t=103)** A million processors might be able to execute the parallel portion of the program in a blink of an eye, but that sequential 5% will still take the same amount of time as it would with just one processor.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/amdahl-s-law?u=76281980&t=117)** This chart shows the estimated speed up that can be achieved when 95% of a program can be parallelized.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/amdahl-s-law?u=76281980&t=125)** As the number of processors is increased from left to right, the speed up rises until it eventually maxes out at 20, if only 90% of a program can be parallelized, then at best we'll get a speed up of 10.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/amdahl-s-law?u=76281980&t=140)** A 75% parallelizable program has a maximum speed up of 4.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/amdahl-s-law?u=76281980&t=147)** And if only 50% of a program can be executed in parallel, then even with an infinite number of processors, the best we can achieve is a measly speed up of two.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/amdahl-s-law?u=76281980&t=158)** If that's all we can get, then we might decide that it's not worth the effort to write the program so it will run in parallel.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/amdahl-s-law?u=76281980&t=165)** Amdahl's Law illustrates why using multiple processors for parallel computing is only really useful for programs that are highly parallelizable.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/amdahl-s-law?u=76281980&t=175)** - When first learning about parallel programming, it's natural to be excited and want to parallelize everything.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/amdahl-s-law?u=76281980&t=181)** Computers have lots of cores nowadays, so why not make everything as parallel as possible?
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/amdahl-s-law?u=76281980&t=187)** That's a common trap to fall into.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/amdahl-s-law?u=76281980&t=189)** Just because you can write programs to be parallel doesn't mean you always should because the cost and overhead associated with parallelization can sometimes outweigh the benefits.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/amdahl-s-law?u=76281980&t=200)** Amdahl's Law, is one handy tool to estimate the benefits of parallelizing a program to determine whether or not it makes sense to do so.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Versions:** 1.9 (1), 2.7 (1), 3.5 (1), 19.6 (1)
> **Speakers:** - there (1), - 95 (1), - when (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (2)
> **CLI Commands:** make (1)
> **Code Keywords:** let (1)

#### Measure speedup
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup?u=76281980&t=0)** - Amdahl's law is great for estimating the speedup that you might achieve by parallelizing a program.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup?u=76281980&t=6)** But once you've created that parallel program, it can be useful to actually measure its speedup empirically.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup?u=76281980&t=12)** Speedup is calculated as the ratio of two values, the time it takes for the program to execute sequentially, divided by the time it takes to execute in its parallel implementation.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup?u=76281980&t=24)** That means we'll actually need to take two separate measurements to calculate speedup.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup?u=76281980&t=28)** First, we'll see how long the algorithm takes to execute with a single processor.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup?u=76281980&t=33)** Now, this doesn't mean just take the parallel version of the program and run it with only one processor because the parallel program will include additional overhead that isn't necessary to run sequentially.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup?u=76281980&t=45)** We want to measure the best possible implementation of that algorithm written with sequential execution in mind.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup?u=76281980&t=51)** - I've got my stopwatch ready.
>

>

>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup?u=76281980&t=76)** - Great. That's our sequential baseline.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup?u=76281980&t=78)** Now, let's try that again working together using a divide-and-conquer approach that's structured - Okay.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup?u=76281980&t=83)** - for parallel execution. - Ready, set, go.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup?u=76281980&t=85)** (receipts rustling) (table tapping)
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup?u=76281980&t=88)** - Done. $103. - 17 seconds this time around.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup?u=76281980&t=93)** - Cool.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup?u=76281980&t=93)** We can calculate speedup now.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup?u=76281980&t=95)** 25 seconds divided by 17 seconds gives us a speedup of 1.47.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup?u=76281980&t=102)** Working together in parallel made us almost 1-1/2 times faster.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup?u=76281980&t=106)** - Not too shabby considering there are only two of us, But if this had been a result with many more processors to help with the work, then a speedup of only 1.47 doesn't sound that impressive.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup?u=76281980&t=117)** - True. Speedup is a great metric, but it doesn't paint the whole picture.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup?u=76281980&t=122)** Another metric to consider is efficiency, which indicates how well system resources, like additional processors, are utilized.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup?u=76281980&t=131)** We can get a rough calculation for efficiency by dividing the speedup by the number of processors.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup?u=76281980&t=137)** So, with just two processors, Olivia and me, we achieved a speedup of 1.47, which means we were 73.5% efficient.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup?u=76281980&t=146)** And I think that's pretty good.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup?u=76281980&t=149)** Now, let's say we increase the number of processors in our system to eight, but doing so only produces a speedup of 2.2.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup?u=76281980&t=158)** Now, we're only running at 27.5% efficiency.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup?u=76281980&t=162)** Our program did not scale well to utilize those additional processors.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup?u=76281980&t=167)** Measuring speedup and efficiency gives us a sense of how well our parallel program is actually performing.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup?u=76281980&t=173)** As long as you achieve a speedup that's greater than 1, you know you've achieved at least something by making the program parallel.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup?u=76281980&t=180)** - But if your speedup is less than 1, you're better off just running the sequential algorithm.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup?u=76281980&t=186)** - Now, a few recommended things to consider when benchmarking a program's performance: It's important to limit the number of programs running at the same time so they don't compete with the program you're trying to measure for resources like processor time.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup?u=76281980&t=200)** Also, since execution scheduling and other background tasks, like garbage collection, can change how long a program takes from run to run, I like to measure the execution time for multiple independent runs of the program and then average those times together.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup?u=76281980&t=216)** Finally, some programming environments use just in time compilation to compile parts of the program at runtime, that can cause the program to execute slower, when it first starts up, so you want to let the environment warm up before you begin taking measurements.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup?u=76281980&t=233)** Some programming languages may have compiler options or runtime settings that can address those concerns, but as a very simple warmup, I always like to run the algorithm I'm going to be measuring once before I actually run and measure it to make sure things like the cache are in a somewhat consistent state from run to run.

>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=47)** On line 31, we have a variable to indicate the number of evaluation runs for each implementation to execute and measure the execution times, and then we'll average those times together.
>

>

>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=86)** Lines 34 to 42 contain code to measure the sequential execution time.
>

>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=106)** At the beginning of each run, we capture the current system time with the start time variable, then execute the sequential algorithm, and when it's done, we calculate the elapsed time and add it to the accumulator.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=120)** Finally, after all those test runs, we divide the accumulated sequential time by the number of eval runs on line 42 to get the average.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=130)** Now, one more thing to point out here.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=133)** Before we actually begin timing anything, we run the sequential sum function on line 36 and capture its resulting output.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=142)** Doing this serves as a rudimentary way to warm up the system.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=148)** The next block of code on lines 44 through 52 accomplishes the same thing but for the parallel algorithm instead.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=157)** After those parallel evaluation runs complete, the final block of code displays the results.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=164)** The if statement on line 55 checks to make sure that the sequential and parallel algorithms produce the same result.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=171)** If they did not, then something has gone wrong and it displays an error message.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=177)** Then the last four print statements display the average sequential and parallel execution times along with the corresponding speed up in efficiency based on the number of processors in the system.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=189)** Now, when I run this program, we see a message that it's evaluating the sequential implementation.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=196)** Then after a bit, it evaluates the parallel implementation and then the results pop up.
>

>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=213)** That corresponds to a speed up of 3.58 and an efficiency of 89.47%, which is calculated based on the four logical processors in this computer.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=226)** With those results in hand, we can try making adjustments or tweak our parallel algorithm and then run the benchmark again to see how it affects the performance.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=237)** For example, we've seen how our parallel algorithm compares to the sequential algorithm when summing together a 100,000,000 numbers, but how do they compare if we need to sum a much smaller range of numbers?

> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=251)** To find out, let's decrease the sum value on line 32 from a 100,000,000 to just 100,000.

> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=260)** Now, if I make and run the program with that change, we see that the parallel algorithm is actually less efficient than the sequential algorithm for the smaller input range.

> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/measure-speedup-c-plus-plus-demo?u=76281980&t=274)** This is because of the additional overhead in the parallel algorithm needed to spawn multiple threads.

### 10. Designing Parallel Programs

#### Partitioning
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/partitioning?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/partitioning?u=76281980&t=0)** - We've looked at a lot of mechanisms for implementing concurrent and parallel programs and considered the concepts and challenges associated with them.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/partitioning?u=76281980&t=9)** Now it's time for the big question.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/partitioning?u=76281980&t=11)** How do you actually design a parallel program?
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/partitioning?u=76281980&t=14)** Over the next few videos, we'll look at a common four step methodology for taking a problem and developing a parallel solution for it.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/partitioning?u=76281980&t=22)** This methodology can be used to design complex programs that run on large scale parallel systems.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/partitioning?u=76281980&t=28)** And not all parts of it are applicable to writing simple desktop applications, like we've done in this course, but the concepts are still good to understand.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/partitioning?u=76281980&t=37)** The four stages can be summarized as partitioning, communication, agglomeration, and mapping.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/partitioning?u=76281980&t=45)** That first stage partitioning, is about breaking down the problem into discreet chunks of work that can be distributed to multiple tasks.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/partitioning?u=76281980&t=54)** At this beginning stage, we're not concerned with practical issues like the number of processors in our computer.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/partitioning?u=76281980&t=60)** We'll consider that later.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/partitioning?u=76281980&t=62)** For now, our goal is to simply decompose the problem at hand into as many small tasks as possible, and there are two basic ways to approach partitioning.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/partitioning?u=76281980&t=71)** Domain decomposition and functional decomposition.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/partitioning?u=76281980&t=75)** Domain, or data decomposition, focuses on dividing the data associated with the problem into lots of small, and if possible, equally sized partitions.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/partitioning?u=76281980&t=86)** The secondary focus is then to consider the computations to be performed and associating them with that partition data.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/partitioning?u=76281980&t=93)** For example, if Olivia and I need to decorate this tray of cupcakes, that's a two dimensional array of data elements we need to process.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/partitioning?u=76281980&t=101)** So, we can use domain decomposition to split that work into two similar tasks.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/partitioning?u=76281980&t=107)** We could break the array into two blocks.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/partitioning?u=76281980&t=110)** I'll handle decorating this half, and you take the other block.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/partitioning?u=76281980&t=113)** - Or we could break up elements cyclically and each decorate every other cupcake.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/partitioning?u=76281980&t=118)** - Sure, that's just another way to break up this data, and different ways of decomposing data can have different advantages and disadvantages depending on the problem and hardware involved.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/partitioning?u=76281980&t=129)** Once we've partitioned the data, we can turn our focus towards the processing that needs to be applied to each section.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/partitioning?u=76281980&t=136)** - The other form of decomposition, functional decomposition, provides a different complimentary way to break down the problem.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/partitioning?u=76281980&t=144)** Rather than focusing on the data being manipulated, functional decomposition begins by considering all of the computational work that a program needs to do, and then divides that into separate tasks that perform different portions of the overall work.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/partitioning?u=76281980&t=158)** The data requirements for those tasks are a secondary consideration.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/partitioning?u=76281980&t=163)** For example, to functionally decompose making cupcakes, we would first break out all of the individual tasks that go into making them.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/partitioning?u=76281980&t=171)** Then from there, we continue on to consider the data involved with each of those tasks.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/partitioning?u=76281980&t=177)** Keep in mind that domain and functional decomposition are complimentary ways to approach a problem, and it's natural to use a combination of the two.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/partitioning?u=76281980&t=186)** Programmers typically start with domain decomposition because it forms a foundation for a lot of parallel algorithms.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/partitioning?u=76281980&t=194)** But sometimes taking a functional approach instead can provide different ways of thinking about these problems.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/partitioning?u=76281980&t=201)** It's worth taking the time to explore alternative perspectives that can reveal problems or opportunities for better optimization that will be missed by considering data alone.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Speakers:** - we (1), - or (1), - sure (1), - the (1)
> **Analogies:** for example (2)
> **Code Keywords:** continue (1)
> **Warnings:** keep in mind (1)

#### Communication
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/communication?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/communication?u=76281980&t=0)** - After decomposing the problem into separate tasks, the next step in our design process is to establish communication, which involves figuring out how to coordinate execution and share data between the tasks.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/communication?u=76281980&t=13)** - Hang on a sec.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/communication?u=76281980&t=14)** Do we always need communication?
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/communication?u=76281980&t=17)** - Well, my dear, communication is the foundation of a good relationship.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/communication?u=76281980&t=21)** - Yeah, yeah, but I was talking about data.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/communication?u=76281980&t=24)** Some problems can be decomposed in ways that do not require tasks to share data between them.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/communication?u=76281980&t=30)** Consider the job of frosting these cupcakes.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/communication?u=76281980&t=33)** If I'm tasked to add frosting to this one and you're tasked to add frosting to that one, even though we're operating on adjacent elements in this array, there's no need for us to communicate with each other.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/communication?u=76281980&t=45)** They're completely independent tasks.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/communication?u=76281980&t=47)** This is embarrassingly easy to make parallel.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/communication?u=76281980&t=50)** - Sure, we could spend our quality family time together in the kitchen not talking to each other, but what if there is a need to share data between tasks?
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/communication?u=76281980&t=59)** Let's say we want to decorate the cupcakes to have a rainbow pattern across them.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/communication?u=76281980&t=64)** That would require each task to know information about the color of its neighboring cupcakes.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/communication?u=76281980&t=69)** I need to know what color you're making your cupcakes so I can color my cupcakes accordingly.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/communication?u=76281980&t=74)** Although our separate tasks can execute concurrently, we're no longer completely independent from each other.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/communication?u=76281980&t=81)** In this type of situation, we might establish a network of direct point-to-point communication links between neighboring tasks.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/communication?u=76281980&t=88)** For each link, one task is acting as the sender or producer of data, and the other task that needs it is the receiver or consumer.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/communication?u=76281980&t=97)** That type of local point-to-point communication can work when each task only needs to communicate with a small number of other tasks.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/communication?u=76281980&t=105)** - But if your tasks need to communicate with a larger audience, then you should consider other structures for sharing data between multiple tasks.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/communication?u=76281980&t=114)** You might have one task that broadcasts the same data out to all members of a group or collective, or it scatters different pieces of the data out to each of the members to process.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/communication?u=76281980&t=125)** Afterwards, that task can gather all of the individual results from the members of the group and combine them for a final output.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/communication?u=76281980&t=133)** When operations require this type of global communication, it's important to consider how it can grow and scale.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/communication?u=76281980&t=139)** Simply establishing point-to-point pairs may not be sufficient.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/communication?u=76281980&t=144)** If one task is acting as a centralized manager to coordinate operations with a group of distributed workers, as the number of workers grow, the communication workload of the central manager grows too and may turn it into a bottleneck.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/communication?u=76281980&t=159)** This is where strategies like divide and conquer can be useful to distribute the computation and communication in a way that reduces the burden on any one task.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/communication?u=76281980&t=169)** These are just a few high-level structures to serve as a starting point as you begin to plan the communications for a parallel program.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/communication?u=76281980&t=177)** - A few other factors to consider include whether the communications will be synchronous or asynchronous.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/communication?u=76281980&t=184)** Synchronous communications are sometimes called blocking communications because all tasks involved have to wait until the entire communication process is completed to continue doing other work.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/communication?u=76281980&t=195)** That can potentially result in tasks spending a lot of time waiting on communications instead of doing useful work.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/communication?u=76281980&t=203)** Asynchronous communications, on the other hand, are often referred to as non-blocking communications because after a task sends an asynchronous message, it can begin doing other work immediately regardless of when the receiving task actually gets that message.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/communication?u=76281980&t=220)** You should also consider the amount of processing overhead a communication strategy involves, because the computer cycles spent sending and receiving data are cycles not being spent processing it.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/communication?u=76281980&t=232)** Latency is another factor to consider, the time it takes for a message to travel from point A to B expressed in units of time like microseconds, and bandwidth, which is the amount of data that can be communicated per unit of time, expressed in some unit of bytes per second.
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/communication?u=76281980&t=250)** Now, if you're just writing basic multithreaded or multiprocess programs to run on a desktop computer, some of these factors like latency and bandwidth probably aren't major concerns because everything is running on the same physical system.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/communication?u=76281980&t=265)** But as you develop larger programs that distribute their processing across multiple physical systems, those intersystem communication factors can have a significant impact on the overall performance.

> **Code Keywords:** require (3), let (1), continue (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (1)

#### Agglomeration
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/agglomeration?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/agglomeration?u=76281980&t=0)** - In the first two stages of our parallel design process, we partitioned a problem into a set of separate tasks and then established communication to provide those tasks with the data they needed.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/agglomeration?u=76281980&t=12)** We looked at different ways to decompose the problem and focused on defining as many small tasks as possible.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/agglomeration?u=76281980&t=20)** That approach helped us consider a wide range of opportunities for parallel execution.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/agglomeration?u=76281980&t=25)** However, the solution it created is not very efficient, especially if there are way more tasks than there are processors on the target computer.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/agglomeration?u=76281980&t=35)** - Now it's time to turn our thinking from abstract to something concrete and modify that design to execute more efficiently on a specific computer.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/agglomeration?u=76281980&t=45)** In the third agglomeration stage, we'll revisit the decisions we made during the partitioning and communication stages to consider changes to make our program more efficient, combining some of those tasks and possibly replicating data or computations.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/agglomeration?u=76281980&t=60)** As a parallel program executes, periods of time spent performing useful computations are usually separated by periods of communication and synchronization events.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/agglomeration?u=76281980&t=71)** The concept of granularity gives us a qualitative measure of the time spent performing computation over the time spent on communication.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/agglomeration?u=76281980&t=80)** Parallelism can be classified into two categories based on the amount of work performed by each task.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/agglomeration?u=76281980&t=87)** With fine-grained parallelism, a program is broken down into a large number of small tasks.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/agglomeration?u=76281980&t=94)** The benefit is that lots of small tasks can be more evenly distributed among processors to maximize their usage; a concept called load balancing.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/agglomeration?u=76281980&t=103)** The downside is that having lots of tasks increases the overhead for communication and synchronization, so it has a lower computation to communication ratio.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/agglomeration?u=76281980&t=115)** On the other end of the spectrum, coarse-grained parallelism splits the program into a small number of large tasks.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/agglomeration?u=76281980&t=123)** The advantage is that it has a much lower communication overhead, so more time can be spent on computation.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/agglomeration?u=76281980&t=130)** However, the larger chunks of work may produce a load imbalance where certain tasks process the bulk of data while others remain idle.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/agglomeration?u=76281980&t=139)** Those are two extremes, and the most efficient solution will be dependent on the algorithm and the hardware on which it runs.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/agglomeration?u=76281980&t=146)** For most general purpose computers, that's usually in the middle with some form of medium-grained parallelism.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/agglomeration?u=76281980&t=153)** - When we partitioned our cupcakes earlier, we took a fine grained approach.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/agglomeration?u=76281980&t=157)** The array has 12 elements that need to be frosted, so we decomposed that into 12 separate tasks; one for each cupcake.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/agglomeration?u=76281980&t=165)** As we evaluated communication, we determined that each cupcake task will need to share data with the four other cupcakes surrounding it to coordinate their colors to form a rainbow pattern, which would require 34 communication events.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/agglomeration?u=76281980&t=181)** In addition to that being a lot of communication, 12 tasks is way more than the number of processors in our kitchen.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/agglomeration?u=76281980&t=188)** There's only two of us.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/agglomeration?u=76281980&t=190)** - Then let's agglomerate and combine some of those tasks.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/agglomeration?u=76281980&t=193)** Since we only have two processors, Olivia and me, we'll restructure the program into two tasks that are each responsible for frosting six of the cupcakes.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/agglomeration?u=76281980&t=204)** That reduces the amount of communications between those tasks from 34 down to just two, because everything else is handled locally within the task.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/agglomeration?u=76281980&t=213)** However, now each time they communicate, they'll have to share more information to convey the status of the three cupcakes along that edge.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/agglomeration?u=76281980&t=221)** Now we have two tasks and two processors. Perfect.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/agglomeration?u=76281980&t=226)** - Hey guys, I heard you need some help frosting cupcakes.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/agglomeration?u=76281980&t=229)** - Oh, Steve.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/agglomeration?u=76281980&t=231)** We just restructured our program into two tasks, and now with Steve, we have three processors available, and that's too many cooks.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/agglomeration?u=76281980&t=240)** - Too many cooks?
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/agglomeration?u=76281980&t=241)** - Too many cooks.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/agglomeration?u=76281980&t=245)** One of us would be sitting idle while the other two cooks are busy processing.
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/agglomeration?u=76281980&t=250)** It's easy to make short-sighted decisions like this that can limit a program's scalability.
>
> **[4:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/agglomeration?u=76281980&t=255)** Choosing to restructure our program into just two tasks prevented us from taking advantage of Steve's additional processing power.
>
> **[4:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/agglomeration?u=76281980&t=262)** A well-designed parallel program should adapt to changes in the number of processors, so keep flexibility in mind.
>
> **[4:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/agglomeration?u=76281980&t=269)** Try not to incorporate unnecessary hardcoded limits on the number of tasks in a program.
>
> **[4:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/agglomeration?u=76281980&t=275)** If possible, use compile time or runtime parameters to control the granularity.
