#### Mapping
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mapping?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mapping?u=76281980&t=0)** - The fourth and final stage of our parallel design process is mapping, and this is where we specify where each of the tasks we established will actually execute.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mapping?u=76281980&t=10)** Now, this mapping stage does not apply if you're only using a single processor system, because there's only one place to execute the program, or if you're using a system with automated task scheduling.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mapping?u=76281980&t=23)** So if I'm just writing programs to run on a desktop computer, like the examples we've shown you throughout this course, mapping isn't even a consideration.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mapping?u=76281980&t=32)** The operating system handles scheduling threads to execute on specific processor cores, so that's out of our hands.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mapping?u=76281980&t=39)** Mapping really becomes a factor if you're using a distributed system or specialized hardware with lots of parallel processors for large-scale problems, like in scientific computing applications.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mapping?u=76281980&t=51)** The usual goal of a mapping algorithm is to minimize the total execution time of the program, and there are two main strategies to achieve that goal.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mapping?u=76281980&t=61)** You can place tasks that are capable of executing concurrently on different processors to increase the overall concurrency, or you can focus on placing tasks that communicate with each other frequently on the same processor to increase locality by keeping them close together.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mapping?u=76281980&t=80)** In some situations, it might be possible to leverage both of those approaches, but more often they'll conflict with each other, which means the design will have to make trade-offs.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mapping?u=76281980&t=89)** There's a variety of different load-balancing algorithms that use domain decomposition and agglomeration techniques to map task execution to processors.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mapping?u=76281980&t=99)** If the number of tasks or the amount of computation and communication per task changes as the program executes, that makes the problem more complex, and it may require dynamic load balancing techniques that periodically determine a new mapping strategy.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mapping?u=76281980&t=115)** Designing a good mapping algorithm is highly dependent on both the program's structure and the hardware it's running on, and that gets beyond the scope of this course.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mapping?u=76281980&t=125)** So to summarize the four-step parallel design process, we start by taking a problem and partitioning or decomposing it into a collection of tasks.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mapping?u=76281980&t=136)** Then we evaluate the communication necessary to synchronize and share data between those tasks.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mapping?u=76281980&t=142)** After that, we agglomerate or combine those tasks into groups to increase the program's efficiency with certain hardware in mind.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/mapping?u=76281980&t=150)** And then finally, those tasks get mapped to specific processors to actually execute.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** require (1), finally, (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (1)
> **Speakers:** - the (1)

#### Challenge: Matrix multiply
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-matrix-multiply?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=0)** (bright electronic music)
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=6)** - [Instructor] Your goal for this challenge is to design and build a parallel program that calculates the product of two matrices, which is a common mathematical operation that can benefit a lot from parallel computation.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=19)** Each matrix will be represented in C++ as a two-dimensional array of arrays.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=25)** The first index corresponds to rows of the matrix, and is usually represented with the variable letter i, and to the second index corresponds to columns, represented with the letter j.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=37)** So, for example, if the first index value is zero, that refers to the top row of the matrix.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=44)** And if the second value is two, that identifies the element at index two along that top row.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=51)** It's common to represent the individual elements of a matrix using notation with subscripts like this, with the first subscript indicating the row, and to the second subscript indicating the column.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=64)** When it comes to multiplication, two matrices can be multiplied together if the number of columns in the first matrix, A, is equal to rows in the second matrix, B.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=75)** So, for example, matrix A shown here is a 4 x 2 matrix, and B is a 2 x 3 matrix.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=83)** Since those inner dimensions are the same, they can be multiplied together.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=88)** The product of A times B, which we'll call matrix C on the right, will have dimensions based on the number of rows in A and to the number of columns in B.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=99)** Each element in matrix C is the product of the corresponding row in A and column in B.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=105)** So, for example, element C2,1 is the product of row 2 from Matrix A and column 1 from B.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=116)** Written as an equation, C2,1 equals A2,0 times B0,1, plus A2,1 times B1,1.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=127)** If we replace the subscripts in that equation with the variables I and J, it's a little easier to see how the first index I corresponds to the row in A and the second index J corresponds to a column in B.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=143)** That's the end of our short math lesson on how matrix multiplication works.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=147)** To give you a starting point for this challenge, we've created this example program.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=153)** On line eight, we've implemented a sequential version of matrix multiplication with the sequential matrix multiply function, which takes in double pointers to the array of arrays representing the two matrices to multiply, A and B, along with a third array of arrays C to hold the result.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=172)** It also has parameters for the number of rows and columns in A and B.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=177)** The function uses a set of nested for loops to iterate through each of the rows in A and columns in B.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=184)** It initializes the corresponding cell in the result array C to zero, and then uses a third for loop on line 14 to sum together the products of elements from the row in A with the column in B and accumulates the sum in the corresponding location of the result array C.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=203)** So the program is modifying the given array C in place to fill it with the result values.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=211)** Below our sequential matrix multiplier is another empty function on line 22 named parallel matrix multiply, and that's where you come in.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=220)** Your goal for this challenge is to implement your own parallelized version of matrix multiplication that will hopefully execute faster than the sequential version.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=229)** Feel free to create any additional helper functions you need as part of your parallel solution.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=235)** Down in the program's main function, we've implemented our simple framework for measuring a parallel program speed up, which we covered in a previous video.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=245)** Lines 31 through 35 specify the number of times to evaluate each algorithm along with the dimensions for input matrices A and B.
>
> **[4:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=255)** Below that, we allocate memory to store the input and result matrices and populate the elements of A and B with random values.

#### Solution: Matrix multiply
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-matrix-multiply?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] To design our parallel solution for the matrix multiplication challenge, we began with domain decomposition to consider ways that we could partition this problem.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=16)** One very convenient aspect of matrix multiplication is that every element in the result matrix C can be calculated independently.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=25)** For example, calculating element C two one only requires knowledge of row two from matrix A and column one from matrix B.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=35)** Likewise, calculating C zero two only requires row zero from A and column two from B.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=43)** The elements of matrix C don't need to know anything about any other elements in C.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=49)** So calculating the individual elements of C for a four by three result matrix can be partitioned into 12 independent tasks.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=58)** This type of problem is sometimes called embarrassingly parallel because it breaks apart so easily and doesn't require communication between each of the tasks.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=69)** Now that can turn into a lot of tasks, especially for a large result matrix.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=74)** So we decided to agglomerate those tasks based on row and we'll modify the number of rows in each group at runtime based on the number of processors that are available in the computer.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=86)** If the system only has two processors, then we'll combine the computations into two tasks.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=92)** If it has four processors, then that's four tasks and so on.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=97)** Our matrix multiplication solution has two parts.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=101)** The parallel matrix multiply function on line 25 and a helper function below it on line 45 named parallel worker, which is responsible for calculating the results for a subset of rows in the total solution matrix.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=115)** Looking at the primary parallel matrix multiply function on line 28, we get the number of available processors in this computer, which we use on the next line to divide the rows of the output matrix into equal size chunks.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=130)** Line 31 creates an array to hold the worker threads, and then the for loop on line 32 starts worker threads to independently calculate and populate a subset of the overall result from a certain start and end row of the result array C.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=148)** The parallel worker function below that on line 45 uses a set of three nested for loops similar to the sequential matrix multiplication algorithm.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=157)** We just changed the indices on line 48 to process a subset of the rows in A, so that's how our solution works.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=166)** Down in the program's main function, we'll be multiplying together two matrices that are 1000 by 1000 elements each.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=174)** Now, before I run this program, I'll press control shift escape to bring up the Task Manager and select the performance tab so we can see the CPU performance.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=185)** Then I'll use the console to start the program, and as the sequential implementation runs, we can see that the CPU usage stays around 25 to 30% because we're only using one of the four processors in this computer.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=205)** This sequential algorithm may take a while, so let's fast forward.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=211)** Now, when the parallel algorithm begins running, the CPU usage spikes up to 100% because now we're utilizing all four of the available processors.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=222)** When the program finishes, we see that the parallel algorithm produced a speed up of 2.78 compared to the sequential version, which corresponds to a 69% efficiency with my four processors, I'd say that's pretty good.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=238)** Now we'll only see these type of good results if the input matrices are large enough.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=243)** If we reduce the size of the matrices from being 1000 by 1000 to just 10 by 10,
>
> **[4:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=254)** build and then run that program again, it completes much faster, but the speed up we get ends up being less than one because the overhead involved in the parallel version makes it less efficient than the sequential algorithm when the problem size is this small.
>
> **[4:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=271)** Our solution is just one way to tackle this problem.
>
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=274)** So if you came up with a different approach, that's great.
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-matrix-multiply?u=76281980&t=277)** Compare it with our solution to understand the advantages and disadvantages of each.

#### Challenge: Merge sort
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-merge-sort?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-merge-sort?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] For our second challenge problem, your goal will be to design and build a parallel version of a classic merge sort algorithm to sort an array of random integers.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-merge-sort?u=76281980&t=18)** Merge sort is a well-known divide and conquer algorithm for sorting the elements in an array.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-merge-sort?u=76281980&t=24)** During the divide phase, it recursively splits the input array into two halves, referred to as the left half and the right half.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-merge-sort?u=76281980&t=33)** From there, the merge phase repeatedly merges those sorted sub arrays to produce new, larger sorted sub arrays, and it continues until there's only one sub array remaining, which is the final sorted result.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-merge-sort?u=76281980&t=50)** To give you a starting point for this challenge, we've already implemented a sequential version of a merge sort algorithm in this example program.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-merge-sort?u=76281980&t=59)** The sequential_merge_sort function on line 10 takes in a pointer to the array of integers that we want to be sorted along with the arguments for the first and last indices of the section of the array to sort, called left and right.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-merge-sort?u=76281980&t=74)** If the left index is less than the right index, that means there are still multiple items in the array to be sorted, so it needs to be divided further.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-merge-sort?u=76281980&t=84)** Line 12 calculates the middle index between the left and right points.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-merge-sort?u=76281980&t=89)** Then the sequential_merge_sort function recursively calls itself to sort the left half of the array from the left to middle index, and then the right half of the array from the next item after the middle up to the right index.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-merge-sort?u=76281980&t=105)** Finally, it calls the merge function on line 15 to merge the two sorted sub arrays.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-merge-sort?u=76281980&t=112)** Scrolling down, merge is where the magic really happens in this program.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-merge-sort?u=76281980&t=118)** The merge function takes in the left, middle, and right indices, which indicate the left and right subsections of the array to merge.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-merge-sort?u=76281980&t=127)** When the merge function gets called, both of these two subsections will already be individually sorted, and the merge function combines and sorts them together into a single sorted section.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-merge-sort?u=76281980&t=140)** Feel free to examine the code behind the merge function on line 28, but to use it, you don't really need to know how it works under the hood.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-merge-sort?u=76281980&t=149)** The one key thing to understand is that it merges sorted values into the original array, and that means this merge sort algorithm is sorting the array in place.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-merge-sort?u=76281980&t=161)** The array that gets passed as a pointer to the array argument in the merge sort function will be modified.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-merge-sort?u=76281980&t=168)** Now at line 20, there's an empty function called parallel_merge_sort.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-merge-sort?u=76281980&t=173)** Your job for this challenge is to implement your own parallelized version of the merge sort function.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-merge-sort?u=76281980&t=180)** Feel free to create any helper functions you need and to reuse sections of code from the sequential version.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-merge-sort?u=76281980&t=187)** In particular, we recommend reusing our merge sort function to tackle this challenge.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-merge-sort?u=76281980&t=194)** Down in this program's main function, we've implemented our simple framework for measuring a parallel program's speed up.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-merge-sort?u=76281980&t=201)** So you can evaluate how well your parallel program performs compared to the sequential version.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-merge-sort?u=76281980&t=208)** Good luck.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** function (12), new, (1), finally, (1)

#### Solution: Merge sort
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-merge-sort?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-merge-sort?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] For our solution to the merge_sort challenge, we used a recursive divide and conquer approach with multiple threads sorting subsections of the overall array.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-merge-sort?u=76281980&t=16)** For the divide phase, rather than recursively subdividing the array until it reaches single elements, we've first configured our base case to subdivide the array based on the number of processors on the computer.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-merge-sort?u=76281980&t=30)** For example, if the computer only had four processors, then it would only go through two layers of subdivisions to produce four sub arrays in need of sorting.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-merge-sort?u=76281980&t=41)** We then give each of the processors a separate thread executing the sequential merge_sort algorithm to sort each sub array in place.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-merge-sort?u=76281980&t=50)** And then the main processor merges the results back together as the recursion unwinds, and each of the other threads finishes sorting their sub arrays.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-merge-sort?u=76281980&t=61)** By limiting the depth of recursion in our base case, we're able to use a few threads to sort large sections of the array, rather than spawning a huge number of new threads that all sort tiny portions of the array.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-merge-sort?u=76281980&t=76)** Our parallel merge_sort function on line 21 has the same three parameters as the sequential algorithm, but we've added a fourth "depth" parameter to track the depth of recursion.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-merge-sort?u=76281980&t=89)** By default, it gets set to zero when the function is first called.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-merge-sort?u=76281980&t=93)** The "if" statement on line 22 represents our base case.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-merge-sort?u=76281980&t=98)** It determines if the recursive calls have reached a sufficient depth based on the number of processors in the system, and if so, it executes the sequential merge_sort algorithm to sort the given section of the array.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-merge-sort?u=76281980&t=113)** Otherwise, the "else" block, starting on line 25, continues to subdivide the array further.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-merge-sort?u=76281980&t=119)** It spawns a separate thread on line 27 to recursively sort the left half of the array, then uses the current thread to sort the right half.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-merge-sort?u=76281980&t=129)** After both of these threads have been sorted and the left thread has rejoined, it calls the merge function on line 30 to merge the two sorted halves together.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-merge-sort?u=76281980&t=140)** It's the exact same merge function we used in the sequential version of the merge_sort.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-merge-sort?u=76281980&t=145)** So that's how our parallel algorithm works.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-merge-sort?u=76281980&t=149)** Down in the main function, our program is configured on line 78 to sort an array holding 100,000 random integers, and it will record the time it takes to run each algorithm a hundred times.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-merge-sort?u=76281980&t=162)** Merge_sort runs quickly, so we can afford to do a large number of eval runs.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-merge-sort?u=76281980&t=168)** Switching over to the console, I'll run the program.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-merge-sort?u=76281980&t=175)** And we see that when sorting a 100,000 element array, our parallel version is roughly 2.28 times faster than the sequential version.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-merge-sort?u=76281980&t=186)** Now, let's try running merge_sort on a much smaller array by decreasing the number of elements on line 78 from 100,000 to just 100.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-merge-sort?u=76281980&t=199)** Then I'll build and run the program.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-merge-sort?u=76281980&t=203)** And not too surprisingly, I see that now the parallel algorithm is much slower than the sequential algorithm due to its additional overhead.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-merge-sort?u=76281980&t=212)** So, somewhere between a 100 and 100,000 is a crossover point where the parallel algorithm becomes faster than the sequential algorithm, at least running on this particular computer.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-merge-sort?u=76281980&t=224)** We could potentially improve our program by doing some benchmarking to find that threshold, and then modify our parallel algorithm to revert to using the sequential algorithm if the input array is below a certain size.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-download-images?u=76281980&t=19)** We've hosted 50 images at the URL shown here, which we'll use as the target files to download for this challenge.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-download-images?u=76281980&t=26)** It's a series of JPEG files, and they're numbered sequentially from one to 50.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-download-images?u=76281980&t=32)** As a starting framework for this challenge, we've implemented a sequential version of an image downloader function at line 13, which has a single input parameter for the number of images to download.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-download-images?u=76281980&t=44)** It simply uses a for loop to repeatedly call the download image helper function on line 16, passing it a different value each time from one to the number of images to download.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-download-images?u=76281980&t=56)** That download image function will download the corresponding image file from the internet and then return its file size in bytes, which gets added to an accumulator variable named total bytes.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-download-images?u=76281980&t=67)** At the end, the sequential image downloader function returns the total number of bytes that were downloaded on line 18.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-download-images?u=76281980&t=75)** That download image helper function can be found down the page starting at line 31, along with a support function it uses named write callback starting at line 55.

> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-download-images?u=76281980&t=85)** Since we only have 50 images hosted on our target website, the code on line 33 takes whatever number you pass as the input argument to the download image function, uses the modular operator to force it between one and 50 and uses that to generate a valid image URL to download.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-download-images?u=76281980&t=104)** You don't need to fully understand the details of how this download code works to use it for this challenge.

> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/challenge-download-images?u=76281980&t=117)** Our download image function utilizes libcurl or C-U-R-L depending on who you ask, which is a common library for client side URL transfer, but unfortunately, it's not part of the C++ standard template library.

#### Solution: Download images
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-download-images?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-download-images?u=76281980&t=0)** (lively music)
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-download-images?u=76281980&t=6)** - [Instructor] For our solution to the download images challenge, we considered each call to the download image helper function as separate tasks that we could run asynchronously.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-download-images?u=76281980&t=16)** And since we would need to get the return value with the number of bytes from each of those tasks, it seemed like the perfect use case for futures.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-download-images?u=76281980&t=24)** So, we create a list on line 26 to hold our futures, then use a for loop to start a bunch of asynchronous tasks to execute the download image function for each image and store the resulting feature object in the list.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-download-images?u=76281980&t=38)** After that, a second for loop on line 30 iterates through the list of futures using the get function to retrieve their return values and then adding them to the total bytes accumulator variable.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-download-images?u=76281980&t=50)** Finally, when all of the futures are complete and their individual results added together, the function will return the total number of bytes downloaded on line 34.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-download-images?u=76281980&t=62)** Scrolling down to the main function, on lines 67 and 68, we have the program configured to evaluate each algorithm three times and download 50 images.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-download-images?u=76281980&t=74)** We've already built the program, but before we run it, I'll press ctrl shift escape to bring up the task manager and select the performance tab so we can view the CPU usage.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-download-images?u=76281980&t=85)** Now, I'll start the program.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-download-images?u=76281980&t=91)** And as the sequential implementation is executing, notice that the CPU usage is really low, only a few percent, so it isn't even fully utilizing a single processor.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-download-images?u=76281980&t=102)** However, the wifi usage jumped up, fluctuating around three to five megabits per second.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-download-images?u=76281980&t=109)** This sequential algorithm could take a while, so let's fast forward.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-download-images?u=76281980&t=114)** Now when the parallel implementation starts running, we can see the wifi usage go way up, spiking up to over 70 megabits per second, but the CPU usage was barely affected; it's still utilizing less than a single processor.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-download-images?u=76281980&t=131)** After the program finishes, we see that the parallel algorithm produced a speed up of over 14.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-download-images?u=76281980&t=137)** So, the multi-threaded version is clearly much faster.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-download-images?u=76281980&t=141)** But how did it achieve a processor efficiency greater than a hundred percent?
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-download-images?u=76281980&t=146)** That doesn't seem possible.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-download-images?u=76281980&t=149)** The previous two challenges with matrix multiplication and merge sort were both CPU bound problems.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-download-images?u=76281980&t=156)** As their parallelized code ran, the CPU usage spiked up to a hundred percent because all of the threads we created were running full-time on all of the processors to generate a solution.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-download-images?u=76281980&t=168)** This challenge, on the other hand, is an I/O bound problem.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-download-images?u=76281980&t=173)** The limiting factor in how fast we can download all the images is not the number of processors on our system, it's our internet speed.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-download-images?u=76281980&t=181)** We could add a hundred more processors to this computer, and it wouldn't make a difference because the program barely utilized even one processor.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-download-images?u=76281980&t=190)** Creating asynchronous tasks to run as concurrent threads improved the program's performance because they could respond and do work as their requested data was downloaded and became available, but they would not have benefited much, if any, from being able to execute in parallel.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-parallel-and-concurrent-programming-with-c-plus-plus/solution-download-images?u=76281980&t=207)** We saved this challenge for the end of the course to emphasize the importance of understanding the limiting factors in a problem so you can address the right issues to get the best performance out of your programs.
